# Theories of Punishment {#theories-of-punishment}

![][image1]

**Retribution**

* **Retribution:** ex post view (backward looking, past conduct) that says punishment is justified because people deserve it. Moral blameworthiness\!  
  * Kant: Crimes are moral wrongs because they violate categorical imperatives (the penal law)  
    * Moral blameworthiness  
    * People are not means to an end  
  * Moore: Punishment justified by moral culpability of those who receive it  
  * Morris: Social contract theory  
  * **Since retribution is about moral blameworthiness of an actor, it is inherently more subjective than utilitarianism and allows for a broader time frame to be considered.**

**Utilitarianism**

* **Utilitarian:** ex ante view (forward looking, future behavior) that focuses on consequences of punishment  
  * **Deterrence \-** assumes the evil of punishment and is only valuable to the extent it produces some socially valuable result, i.e. less crime.  
    * **General Deterrence**: Meant to deter general community  
    * **Specific Deterrence**: Meant to deter Dâ€™s	future misconduct specifically  
    * **Ineffective Deterrent** ğŸ¡ª Benefit of crime (to D) **\>** probability of punishment **x** severity of punishment  
    * **Effective deterrent** ğŸ¡ª Benefit of crime (to D) **\<** probability of punishment **x** severity of punishment  
  * **Incapacitation**: Dâ€™s imprisonment physically prevents him from committing a crime  
  * **Rehabilitation:** Dâ€™s punishment prevents crime by altering his behavior  
    * Vitiello, â€œ\[M\]oral imperative to offer the offender a chance at moral transformation.â€

| Elements of Criminal Law |
| :---: |

Common Law Requirements ğŸ¡ª Actus reus (act requirement) \+ Mens rea (mental state)

# ACTUS REUS â€“ General {#actus-reus-â€“-general}

| 		Actus Reus Requirements |
| :---- |
| Punishment for past, voluntary or potentially harmful conduct specified by statute For criminal liability to attach: Must be a voluntary act or omission (must be overt) Criminal conduct must be voluntary (**volition requirement**) Examples of non-voluntary behavior (MPC Â§2.01(2))  ğŸ¡ª reflexes, convulsion, conduct during hypnosis, bodily movements during unconsciousness or sleep  **TIP:** \*Time framing (*Kelman*) here is VERY important. Whether court takes narrow or broad time frame changes what constitutes the voluntary act and whether it is a conduct, attendant circumstance, or result.  |

	

| ACTUS REUS (GENERAL) \- MPC |
| :---- |
|  **MPC Â§ 1.13** In this Code, unless a different meaning plainly is required:  (2) â€œactâ€ or â€œactionâ€ means a bodily movement whether voluntary or involuntary; (9) â€œelement of an offenseâ€ means  **(i) such conduct or**  **(ii) such attendant circumstances or**  **(iii) such a result of conduct as**  (a) is included in the description of the forbidden conduct in the definition of the offense; or (b) establishes the required kind of culpability; or (c) negatives an excuse or justification for such conduct; or (d) negatives a defense under the statute of limitations; or (e) establishes jurisdiction or venue;  |

# Actus reus â€“ Voluntary Acts {#actus-reus-â€“-voluntary-acts}

Criminal liability always requires an â€œactus reusâ€ or the commission or omission of a voluntary act that is prohibited by law.

| ACTUS REUS (VOLUNTARY ACTS) \- MPC |
| :---- |
|  **MPC Â§ 2.01** (1) A person is not guilty of an offense unless his liability is based on conduct which includes a ***voluntary ac*****t** or the omission to perform an act of which he is physically capable. (2) The following are not voluntary acts within the meaning of this Section:  (a) a reflex or convulsion;  (b) a bodily movement during unconsciousness or sleep;  (c) conduct during hypnosis or resulting from hypnotic suggestion;  (d) a bodily movement that otherwise is not a product of the effort or determination of the actor, either conscious or habitual.  |

## Martin v. State (Ala. Ct. App., 1994\) â€“ drunk on highway {#martin-v.-state-(ala.-ct.-app.,-1994)-â€“-drunk-on-highway}

## â€œcriminal liability only attaches for voluntary actsâ€ {#â€œcriminal-liability-only-attaches-for-voluntary-actsâ€}

**Rule**:	Criminal liability may only be imposed when the unlawful conduct is committed voluntarily.

**Facts**:	Police officers arrested Martin (D) at his house and took him to the highway, where he started yelling profanities, indicating a drunken condition. D was convicted of being drunk on a public highway and appealed. 

**Statute:** â€œAny person who, while intoxicated or drunk, appears in any public place where one or more persons are present, . . . 	and manifests a drunken condition by boisterous or indecent conduct, or loud and profane discourse, shall, on 	conviction, be fined.â€

**Holding/Rationale:** Conviction under statute overturned. 

* One cannot be accused of public drunkenness if they were involuntarily brought to a public place by an arresting officer.  
  * By a strict reading of the law, the Dâ€™s conduct appears to meet the letter of the law, but it was not voluntary and thus, no actus reus.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* The court doesnâ€™t get to the question of whether the statute is invalid; though as Harcourt mentioned, the panel is probably skeptical of such criminality. 

## People v. Newton (Cal. Ct. App., 1970\) â€“ BP founder â€œshootsâ€ cop {#people-v.-newton-(cal.-ct.-app.,-1970)-â€“-bp-founder-â€œshootsâ€-cop}

## â€œunconsciousness when physically acting is a complete defense if not self-inducedâ€ {#â€œunconsciousness-when-physically-acting-is-a-complete-defense-if-not-self-inducedâ€}

**Rule**:	Unconsciousness, if not self-induced, as by voluntary intoxication or by the equivalent, is a complete defense to a charge of criminal homicide where a subject physically acts but is not conscious of acting. (Cal Penal Code)

**Facts**:	Newton (BP co-founder) was pulled over by an officer, they struggled with a gun (unsure whoâ€™s gun), the officer was fatally shot and Newton was shot in the abdomen. Newton hospitalized. Testified that he was unconscious between getting shot and being hospitalized. 

**Holding/Rationale:** Conviction reversed. TC committed reversible error by failing to instruct jury on the unconscious   
                      defense. 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Looking at reflex, blacked out due to gunshot wound.   
* Questions to think about  
  * Does it matter who shot whom first? What if Newton wasnâ€™t shot at all? If he wasnâ€™t shot, then no unconsciousness defense.   
  * Is the statute broader or narrower than MPC Â§2.01(2)? Argument can be made that its broader.  
    

## People v. Decina (N.Y., 1956\) â€“ epileptic attack kills 4 {#people-v.-decina-(n.y.,-1956)-â€“-epileptic-attack-kills-4}

## â€œone prior voluntary act in Dâ€™s course of conduct can sufficeâ€ {#â€œone-prior-voluntary-act-in-dâ€™s-course-of-conduct-can-sufficeâ€}

**Rule**:	One may be criminally culpable for involuntary acts caused by a condition if the individual knows of the condition and disregards the possible consequences.

**Facts**:	D was indicted for driving despite knowing he was prone to epileptic attacks that caused him to lose consciousness. Lost consciousness while driving and killed 4 people. 

**Statute:** â€œA person who operates or drives any vehicle of any kind in a reckless or culpably negligent manner, whereby a human being is killed is guilty of criminal negligence in the operation of a vehicle resulting in death.â€

**Holding/Rationale:** This was criminal negligence within the meaning of the statute. 

* Early voluntary act of getting into the car, knowing he suffered epilepsy, satisfied the voluntary act requirement. 


**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Arguments for no voluntary action (D)  
  * Act requirement \= driving a vehicle. When D had a seizure, he was no longer driving the vehicle, and the resulting death was not due to his actions.  
* Argument for voluntary act (State)   
  * Decina getting into the car and driving, prior to his seizure, was a voluntary act.  
* Questions to consider  
  * Is the courtâ€™s analogy of epilepsy to drunk driving convincing?   
  * What if Decina had the most severe form of alcoholism?   
  * Would this qualify as a reflex or convulsion under MPC Â§ 2.01(2)(a)?  
  * Is this even an actus reus case? The court talks about decision to drive with epilepsy and â€œknowledgeâ€ and â€œculpably negligent.â€

# Actus Reus â€“ Omissions  {#actus-reus-â€“-omissions}

## Jones v. U.S. (D.C. Cir. 1962\) â€“ infant dies in mom friendâ€™s care {#jones-v.-u.s.-(d.c.-cir.-1962)-â€“-infant-dies-in-mom-friendâ€™s-care}

## â€œfor crime by omission, duty neglected must be a legal duty that immediately and directly caused deathâ€ {#â€œfor-crime-by-omission,-duty-neglected-must-be-a-legal-duty-that-immediately-and-directly-caused-deathâ€}

**Rule**:	For crime by omission, the duty neglected must be a legal duty, and not a mere moral obligation. It must be a duty imposed by law or contract, and the omission to perform the duty must be the immediate and direct cause of death. **(see notes for 4 situations that result in criminal breach of duty)**

**Facts**:	D was convicted of involuntary manslaughter for failure to provide for a friendâ€™s infant. Unclear whether D agreed to take care of the baby or was compensated. D nonetheless had the financial means to provide food and medical care.  

**Holding/Rationale:**	  
Lower courtâ€™s failure to instruct the jury on neglect of legal duty as a critical element of the crime is plain  
error. Reversed and remanded.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

**Four situations in which the failure to act constitutes breach of legal duty**

1. Statute imposes duty  
2. Stands in certain status relationship to another  
   1. Parent to child  
   2. Husband to wife  
   3. Master to apprentice   
   4. Shipmaster to crew and passengers  
   5. Innkeeper to inebriated   
3. Assumed contractual duty to care for another  
4. Voluntarily assumed the care of another and so secluded the helpless person as to prevent others from rendering aid 

# Mens Rea {#mens-rea}

The **default mens rea is recklessness** \- Â§2.02(3). 

* This is because (1) a prosecutor wouldnâ€™t go for the higher mens rea and make it unnecessarily harder for her case; and (2) a jury wouldnâ€™t need a higher mens rea to convict.  
    
  


| MENS REA \- MPC |
| :---- |
|  **MPC Â§ 1.13** (12) intent \= purpose (16) reasonable belief â‰  reckless or negligent The bar for â€œreasonable beliefâ€ is at least as high as that for â€œknowingly.â€  **MPC Â§ 2.02(2)** (a) Purposely (b) Knowingly (c) Recklessly (d) Negligently  **MPC Â§ 2.02(3)** Default mens rea is recklessness.  **MPC Â§ 2.02(4)** Unless there is a clearly contrary purpose, the mens rea that attaches to the actus reus attaches to the other elements of the crime.  **MPC Â§ 2.02(5)** Proving one mens rea also proves every mens rea â€œbelowâ€ the proven level of culpability.   |

## 

## Regina v. Cunningham (UK, 1957\) â€“ Gas Leak  {#regina-v.-cunningham-(uk,-1957)-â€“-gas-leak}

## â€œmalice wickedness; D must foresee and recklessly disregard harmâ€ {#â€œmalice-wickedness;-d-must-foresee-and-recklessly-disregard-harmâ€}

**Rule**:	The term malice in a criminal statute does not mean general wickedness; it means either (1) an actual intention to do the particular kind of harm that was in fact done or (2) reckless disregard of a foreseeable risk that the harm would result.

**Facts**:	D tore gas meter from wall to get $ and in doing so caused a gas leak leading to the partial asphyxiation of his future mother-in-law. D convicted of maliciously administering a noxious thing after the trial judge instructed that malicious meant wicked.  

**Statute:** â€œWhosoever shall unlawfully and maliciously administer to or cause to be administered to or taken by any other person any poison or other destructive or noxious thing, so as thereby to endanger the life of such person, or so as thereby to inflict upon such person any grievous bodily harm, shall be guilty of felony . . . .â€

**Holding/Rationale:** Trial judge erred in instructing jury that malice meant wickedness. 

* Malice requires more than just wickedness; the defendant must have foreseen and recklessly disregarded the harm. 


**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Negligence is insufficient for a showing of malice ğŸ¡ª see MPC 2.02(2)

## Regina v. Faulkner (UK, 1887\) â€“ Lit Man; Lit Ship  {#regina-v.-faulkner-(uk,-1887)-â€“-lit-man;-lit-ship}

## â€œD canâ€™t be convicted for an accidental act collateral to the felony that would have been a crime if intentionalâ€ {#â€œd-canâ€™t-be-convicted-for-an-accidental-act-collateral-to-the-felony-that-would-have-been-a-crime-if-intentionalâ€}

**Rule**:	A defendant who is in the process of committing a felony cannot be convicted for an accidental act collateral to the felony that would have been a crime if done intentionally.

**Facts**:	D tried to steal rum from a ship, lit a match to see and accidentally set the ship on fire â€“ destroying it. Charged with violating the Malicious Damage Act by â€œmaliciouslyâ€ setting fire to the ship. Trial judge instructed jury that if they found him guilty of theft and believed the fire took place during theft, he should be liable for the fire.   

**Statute:** â€œfeloniously, unlawfully, and maliciouslyâ€ set fire to the ship

**Holding/Rationale:** D cannot be convicted for an act that is the unintended and unforeseeable consequence of a lesser   
        offense. In order to maliciously set fire to the ship, D must have acted intentionally and willfully.   

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Malice requires that the defendant foresaw the disputed consequences 

## State v. Hazelwood (AK, 1887\) â€“ Crim v. ord Neg (oil ranker runs into reef) {#state-v.-hazelwood-(ak,-1887)-â€“-crim-v.-ord-neg-(oil-ranker-runs-into-reef)}

## â€œordinary negligence can be applicable in a criminal caseâ€ {#â€œordinary-negligence-can-be-applicable-in-a-criminal-caseâ€}

**Rule**:	In Alaska, an ordinary negligence standard is constitutionally sufficient to impose criminal punishment for conduct that society seeks to deter.

**Facts**:	D, captain of an oil tanker, ran his ship into a reef. Crash resulted in 11 million gallons of oil spilling into the water.  
**Statute:** It is a misdemeanor to negligently â€œdischarge, cause to be discharged, or permit the discharge of petroleum . . . upon 	the waters or land of the state.â€

**Holding/Rationale:** Ordinary negligence can be applicable in a criminal case. 

* **Criminal negligence requires a risk so great that failing to perceive it constitutes a *gross* deviation from the standard of care that a reasonable person would observe in the situation**  
* A court can apply either standard depending on the reasonableness of the expectation of individual conformity to the standard of care.  
* Here, either standard reasonably deter the criminal behavior.


**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **TA:** But would a conviction and consequent sentence be proportionate to the differing levels of culpability? Think about which standard each side would want to use

## Santillanes v. New Mexico (NM, 1993\) â€“nephewâ€™s neck cut w/ knife {#santillanes-v.-new-mexico-(nm,-1993)-â€“nephewâ€™s-neck-cut-w/-knife}

## â€œwhen moral condemnation attaches to conviction, crime should reflect mens rea that warrants such contemptâ€ {#â€œwhen-moral-condemnation-attaches-to-conviction,-crime-should-reflect-mens-rea-that-warrants-such-contemptâ€}

**Rule**:	**Quimbee:** In New Mexico, the mens rea element of negligence in the stateâ€™s child abuse statute requires a showing of criminal negligence, not ordinary civil negligence.

* Civil negligence is â€œâ€œone which a reasonably prudent person would foresee as involving an unreasonable risk of injury to himself or to another and which such a person, in the exercise of ordinary care, would not do.â€

  **Facts**:	D, cut his 7-year-old nephewsâ€™ neck with a knife during an altercation. At trial, the judge instructed the jury that civil negligence applied. He was convicted of child abuse. 


**Statute:** Child abuse is â€œnegligently causing a child to be placed in a situation that may endanger the childâ€™s life or health.â€

**Holding/Rationale:** The requisite culpability for a criminal act is higher than that of a civil tort. 

* When moral condemnation and social opprobrium (shame) attach to a conviction of a crime, the crime should reflect a mental state warranting such contempt.  
  * The culpability should reflect the seriousness of the crime. 


  


### Mens Rea: Distinguishing *Hazelwood* From *Santillanes*

* Statutory language shows two crimes of varying degrees of seriousness   
* *Hazelwood* (oil tanker crash â€“ picked ord. neg.)  
  * Petroleum discharge can be legal with permit/license  
  * Typical criminal actors might be experts/experienced and thus can be held to a higher standard of care (lower bar for criminality \= less leeway in deviation from care \= higher standard)  
* *Santillanes* (child abuse â€“ picked crim neg.)  
  * Child abuse carries with it a lot of moral baggage.   
  * Despite the moral baggage, â€œendangering the childâ€™s life or healthâ€ without more specificity would cover a lot of conduct. Do we want to criminalize every negligent act that endangers a child (this could happen if we used a lower standard)? 

## Elonis v. United States (2015) â€“ threats via facebook {#elonis-v.-united-states-(2015)-â€“-threats-via-facebook}

## â€œthere must be a mens rea attached to wrongful conduct, even if not explicitly stated in statuteâ€ {#â€œthere-must-be-a-mens-rea-attached-to-wrongful-conduct,-even-if-not-explicitly-stated-in-statuteâ€}

**Rule**:	Criminal statutes generally include a requirement that a person is aware that he or she is committing a crime, even if the statute does not explicitly contain such a mens rea requirement.

**Facts**:	D was convicted of communicating online threats via Facebook posts. Posts included violent rap lyrics.

**Statute:** It is a crime to transmit in interstate commerce â€œany communication containing any threat . . . to injure the person of another.â€ 18 U.S.C. Â§ 875(c).

**Holding/Rationale:** There must be a mens rea attached to the wrongful conduct.

* Just because thereâ€™s no mens rea listed in the statute doesnâ€™t mean that there is none. Criminality requires a minimum of negligence.   
  * Wrongdoing must be conscious to be criminal.   
  * A guilty mind is a necessary element in the indictment and proof of every crime.


**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* When there is no (clear) mens rea, use the statutory text to interpret one into the statute.   
  * If one cannot be read into the statute, apply the default rules of the jx ğŸ¡ª recklessness in MPC-land  \- MPC 2.02(3)  
* **Note:** court suggests that you shouldnâ€™t apply negligence when possible.

**Tips:**

* For this class, it is important to get the big picture. Harcourt will not likely require memorizing the detailed rules of specific jurisdictions.  
* Homicide is all about gradations. In exam, try to find where the fact patterns should be located on this graph.  
* Note there is also justified homicide (self-defense, law enforcement use of force etc.) in this picture, which may be a murder but we donâ€™t attach any liability whatsoever.

**Possible charges for homicide you didnâ€™t commit:**

1. Felony-murder  
2. Accomplice liability for principalâ€™s killing  
3. Conspiracy, then accomplice liability through *Pinkerton*

# Intended Killings: Under the Common Law {#intended-killings:-under-the-common-law}

Recognized 2 broad categories: murder and manslaughter. Both murder and manslaughter can be intentional or unintentional

* **Legislative Grading:** Accomplished by dividing homicidal conduct into crimes of different names (murder v. manslaughter) and by dividing the same crime into different degrees (first degree v. second degree)

**Harcourt Foreword: Law of Homicide** 

* Foucaultâ€™s approach to understanding discourse (applying the historical/ archaeological/ genealogical analysis to social practices and behaviors) can be used to understand premeditation and the different ways courts treat abnormal behavior with respect to whether or not the crime in question was consciously done  
* In *Carroll*, what role does sexuality play in the defenseâ€™s argument? Any implicit or explicit assumptions those theories re their relationship are based on?  
* Same questions in *Guthrie* but from the perspective of mental health   
* **Takeaway:** social norms influence people and the court when they determine whether something is a criminal deviation from the norm

**Common Law (Blackstone) Murder**

* Killing of a human being by another human being with malice aforethought  
  * Aforethought is relevant when thereâ€™s a distinction of premeditation or spur-of-the-moment.

**Common Law First-Degree Murder (PA)**

* Willful, deliberate, and premeditated  
  * Willful \= intent to kill  
  * Deliberate \= no excitement or passion, i.e., not excused   
  * Premeditated \= gray area; debated in *Carroll* and *Guthrie*   
* N.B. PA statute requires specific intent to kill 

**Murder: Homicide with malice aforethought (Blackstone)**

* Actus reus: Killing another without justification, excuse, or mitigating circumstance   
  * Mens rea: killing with â€œmalice aforethoughtâ€   
  * Types of malice aforethought:   
    * Premeditation  
    * The intent to cause death  
    * The intent to inflict grievous bodily injury on another  
    * Depraved heart (an extremely reckless disregard and indifference to the value of human life)   
    * The intent to commit a felony, during the commission or attempted commission of which a death results (aka felony murder: during the course of a felony)  
* Classified by degrees based on willfulness, deliberation and premeditation (**legislative grading)**:  
  * 1st degree: if both intentional and premeditated, or if it involves a killing during the course of another major felony (as defined by statute)  
    * Generally, only 1st degree murder with an aggravating circumstance would have an option for death penalty   
  * 2nd degree: anything not falling within 1st degree 

**Manslaughter \= Homicide without malice aforethought**

* Voluntary manslaughter:   
  * Intentional killing thatâ€™d otherwise be murder but committed in sudden heat of passion as result of adequate provocation without cooling off period  
  * Involuntary manslaughter:   
    * Criminal Negligence: Unintentional killing that results from an act lawful by itself but done in a grossly negligent manner   
      * If committed during a felony, then it is murder   
    * Misdemeanor-Manslaughter: Unintentional killing resulting from commission of an unlawful act not amounting to felony 

      	

## Premeditation {#premeditation}

**Premeditation-Deliberation Formula** 

* Nearly all states that grade murder by degrees provide that â€œ**willful, deliberate, premeditated**â€ killing is murder in the 1st degree.   
  * â€œWillfulâ€ in this context simply means â€œa specific intent to cause death.â€   
* **Purpose**: The legislative division of murder into degrees is meant to separate the most heinous forms of murder, which deserve the most severe penalties, from those which, though intentional in some sense, lack the gravity associated with first degree murders.   
  * **Potential frustration of purpose:** To the extent that these three elements â€“ intent-to-kill, deliberation (careful, thorough, and cool calculation and consideration of effects and consequences of action), and premeditation (form intent to kill) â€“ are not treated as separate, significant elements, the legislative line b/w 1st and 2nd degree murder is lost.  
* ***Guthrie*** **Approach (premeditation takes time) â€“ preserves essence of deliberation**  
  * Courts that take the *Guthrie* approach and provide that it takes some appreciable time to premeditate, preserves the essence of the term â€œdeliberationâ€ by requiring proof that the killer had time not only to form the intent to kill, but also to turn the matter over in her mind and give the matter at least a second thought.   
* **Contrast with *Carroll* approach (no time is too short) that loses essence**  
  * By contrast, courts that take the *Carroll* approach in providing that no time is too short for the necessary premeditation to form, may lose the essence of the term deliberation, which undermines the legislative division of murder into degrees.

## People v. Anderson (CA, 1968\) â€“ sufficient evidence to sustain a finding of premeditation {#people-v.-anderson-(ca,-1968)-â€“-sufficient-evidence-to-sustain-a-finding-of-premeditation}

## â€œ(1) planning activity (2) motive (3) manner. If all 3, usually 1st degree murderâ€ {#â€œ(1)-planning-activity-(2)-motive-(3)-manner.-if-all-3,-usually-1st-degree-murderâ€}

The CA SC explained that evidence sufficient to sustain a finding of premeditation generally falls into three basic categories:

* (1) Facts regarding the Dâ€™s behavior prior to the killing which might indicate a design to take a life **(planning activity)**  
* (2) Facts about the Dâ€™s prior relationship with the victim which might indicate a reason to kill **(â€œmotiveâ€)**  
* (3) Evidence that â€œthe *manner* of the killing was so particular and exacting that the defendant must have intentionally killed according to a **â€˜preconceived design.â€™â€ (manner)**  
  The court added that this court sustains verdicts of first degree murder typically when there is evidence of ***all three types*** and otherwise requires at least extremely strong evidence of (1) or evidence of (2) in conjunction with either (1) or (3)  
    
  	 

## Commonwealth v. Carroll (Pa, 1963\) â€“ No reflection period for 1st degree murder (D shot wife in back of head) {#commonwealth-v.-carroll-(pa,-1963)-â€“-no-reflection-period-for-1st-degree-murder-(d-shot-wife-in-back-of-head)}

## â€œno time is too short for the necessary premeditation to occurâ€ {#â€œno-time-is-too-short-for-the-necessary-premeditation-to-occurâ€}

**Rule**:	The length of time between the formation of the intent and the action does not matter with respect to premeditation. â€œNo time is too shortâ€ for the necessary premeditation to occur. 

**Facts**:	After an argument, D shot his wife in the back of the head (2x) while they were laying in bed. She allegedly harmed their children, and it is disputed whether she suffered from a mental disorder. The gun was placed in the bedroom for the wifeâ€™s safety. Testified that he didnâ€™t â€œknow positivelyâ€ if he intended to murder his wife. 

**Statute:** First degree murder is a willful, deliberate, and premeditated killing. 

**Holding/Rationale:** Court held that there was â€œno doubt that this killing constituted murder in the first degreeâ€. The **short length of time** between planning the murder and killing is irrelevant. All that matters is whether it was â€œ**willful, deliberate, and premeditated.â€** 

* First-degree murder requires **specific intent** to kill, which can be inferred from defendantâ€™s conduct or attendant circumstances.   
  * Premeditation can occur just in the moments before a killing.  
  * Length of time and a general lack of planning doesnâ€™t preclude a finding of premeditation.   
* Psychiatristâ€™s testimony that rage, desperation, and panic produced an â€œimpulsive automatic reflex type of homicideâ€ was belied by defendantâ€™s statements that he remembered the gun, deliberately took it down, and deliberately fired two shots.


  

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **TIP:** Contrast with *Guthrie* (premeditation requires actual reflection)  
* **Policy:** Effectively eliminates any distinction between 1st and 2nd degree murder which goes against the legislatureâ€™s rationale for creating degrees of murder. 


## State v. Guthrie (WV, 1995\) â€“ premed. for 1st degree requires Reflection (Stabbed victim for touching nose) {#state-v.-guthrie-(wv,-1995)-â€“-premed.-for-1st-degree-requires-reflection-(stabbed-victim-for-touching-nose)}

## â€œpremeditation requires actual reflection on the intention to kill after the intent to kill has been formed â€œ {#â€œpremeditation-requires-actual-reflection-on-the-intention-to-kill-after-the-intent-to-kill-has-been-formed-â€œ}

**Rule**:	To constitute first-degree murder, the defendant must have had some period of time between the development of the intent to kill and the actual killing to indicate that the act was premeditated and deliberate and not impulsive.

* There must be evidence indicating that the defendant weighed and considered their decision to kill.   
    
  **Facts**:	After being subjected to heckling by the victim, D grabbed a knife and stabbed the victim. D had identifiable mental disorders and was particularly sensitive about his nose, which the victim flicked prior to the stabbing. D testified o having a panic attack before the killing.  
* D was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to life.  
* The jury was instructed with no meaningful distinction between first- and second-degree murders. 

**Holding/Rationale:** TC erred. Premeditation requires *some* time to contemplate the decision to kill, though there isnâ€™t any set time.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **TIP:** Contrast with *Carroll* (no time is too short for premeditation)   
* Similar statute to *Carroll*: First-degree murder: â€œ\[A\]ny willful, deliberate and premeditated killing.â€   
* **Re issue:** Defense challenged the jury instructions that blurred the line between first- and second-degree murder and instructed that â€œit is only necessary that such intention should have come into existence for the first time at the time of such killing, or any time previously.â€

# Intended Killings: MPC Approach {#intended-killings:-mpc-approach}

* **MPC 210.1(1)**: A person is guilty of criminal homicide if he purposely, knowingly, recklessly or negligently causes the death of another human being.   
* The MPC recognizes 3 categories of criminal homicide â€“ **MPC 210.1(2)**  
  * Murder  
  * Manslaughter  
  * Negligent homicide   
* MPC Sentencing Proposal  
  * 1st degree felony \= max death/life imprisonment  
  * 2nd degree felony \= max 10 years  
  * 3rd degree felony \= not to exceed 10 years   
* The MPC definition of murder abandons the common law element of malice aforethought. Instead, the requisite mens rea is purposely, knowingly, or with recklessness that manifests extreme indifferent to human life   
* Liability for manslaughter cannot be premised on negligence (change from common law) 

| HOMICIDE DEFINITIONS |
| :---- |
| **MPC Â§ 210** (1) human being: born and alive The MPC distinguishes between homicide and feticide. (3) serious bodily injury: substantial risk of death, permanent disfigurement, or protracted loss/impairment of the function of any body part/organ (4) deadly weapon: firearm; or any other thing that is used or is intended to be used to cause death or serious bodily injury Basically, no object is off the table as a deadly weapon so long as the intent is there. |

| MPC 210.2: Criminal homicide constitutes murder (1st degree felony) when killing is:  | MPC 210.3: Criminal homicide constitutes manslaughter (2nd degree felony) when killing is: | MPC 210.4: Criminal homicide constitutes negligent homicide (3rd degree felony) when killing is: |
| :---- | :---- | :---- |
| (a) Committed purposely or knowingly; or (b) Committed recklessly under the circumstances, manifesting extreme indifference to value of life.  Such recklessness (mens rea) is presumed if actor is engaged in, or an accomplice in, commission/attempted commission of robbery, rape or deviated sexual intercourse by force or threat of forceâ€¦ | (a) Committed recklessly; or (b) A homicide which would otherwise be murder is committed under the influence of extreme mental or emotional disturbance for which there is a reasonable explanation or excuse. Reasonableness of such explanation or excuse shall be determined from the viewpoint of a person in the actorâ€™s situation under the circumstances as he believes them to be. **Subjective standard. Very D favorable.** | (1) Committed negligently. D should have known of substantial and unjustifiable risk that his conduct would cause anotherâ€™s death. Gross deviation from the standard of care that a reasonable person would observe in the actorâ€™s situation.  |

## Provocation: Mitigation of Murder to Voluntary Manslaughter {#provocation:-mitigation-of-murder-to-voluntary-manslaughter}

| 		Mitigation to Voluntary Manslaughter Elements |
| :---- |
|   Murder \+ provocation \= voluntary manslaughter  Murder may be mitigated to â€œvoluntary manslaughterâ€ where there was an (1) adequate provocation that  (2) caused the defendant to act in the heat of passion.  Provocation therefore is an excuse or affirmative defense for murder.   |

**POLICY CONSIDERATIONS**

* Possible Rationales for Mitigation:   
  * Under passion, conduct is less reflective and, therefore, less blameworthy (retribution)  
  * Provocation reduces the moral blameworthiness of killing because defendants think they have good reason to kill  
  * From a utilitarian perspective, the law seeks to find an optimal level of deterrence, and those who kill upon provocation are likely not as dangerous as those who kill without.

Three views on what is adequate provocation:

* *Girouard* represents the common law approach (categorical \+ objective).  
  * At common law, reasonable provocation is not caused by words alone and it cools off â€œin a reasonable amount of time.â€   
* *Maher* represents a minority view that some jurisdictions follow (standard \+ subjective).  
* *Casassa* represents the MPC approachâ€”some states have adopted fully; others have revised it

| Categorical (Majority-*Girouard*) *Objective* | Fact-specific (Minority-*Maher*) *Subjective* | Standard (MPC 210.3(1)(b)) *Subjective \+ Objective*  |
| :---- | :---- | :---- |
| Calculated to inflame passion of objective reasonable man; tend to cause him to act from passion rather than reason Suddenness required (no cooling off) Words alone are insufficient to inflame reasonable person  **Words are sufficient only if the words are both** (1) calculated to inflame the passion of a reasonable man and tend to cause him to act for the moment from passion rather than reason; and  (2) accompanied by conduct indicating a present intention and ability to cause the defendant bodily harm. |   Provocation is Q for jury to see if facts as a whole demonstrate sufficient provocation for objective reasonable person   Does not need to occur in Dâ€™s presence; expands adultery provocation |   Extreme mental/emotional disturbance   Reasonableness of excuse Q for jury   Cooling off period allowed   Words alone ok   The question is whether the actorâ€™s loss of self-control can be understood in terms that arouse sympathy in the ordinary citizen?  |
| Limited to:   extreme assault or battery on D   illegal arrest   injury or serious abuse of close relative;   observe spousal adultery   mutual combat |   Any event that inspires objective reasonable Â person to act rashly or w/o due deliberation, and from heat of passion rather than judgment Softens words are not enough rule in *Girouard*  Fear of bodily harm not required   Against categorical approach |   (1\) Subjective Q: was D acting in EMED?   (2) Objective Q: was there reasonable explanation or excuse for EMED, given how D viewed situation?  Reasonable person in Dâ€™s situation  Incorporate Dâ€™s personal handicaps and relevant external characteristics (height, weight, sex, religion, race culture) to assess gravity of provocation; but not factors to assess level of self-control (idiosyncratic moral values, short-temper, alcoholism)  |
| ***Girouard*****:** D stabs wife after she insults him | ***Maher*****:** D heard about wifeâ€™s affair and saw them walk into woods, but did not see them have sex; tried to kill V.  **Harcourt:** this is an arcane understanding of provocation motivated by patriarchal conceptions of a manâ€™s â€œrightâ€ to his woman. | ***Casassa***: obsessed w/ girl and kills her out of EMED Incorporate personal handicaps |

## Girouard v. State (MD., 1991\) â€“ Common Law (MAJ.) Categorical and OBj. (D stabbed wife after she insults him) {#girouard-v.-state-(md.,-1991)-â€“-common-law-(maj.)-categorical-and-obj.-(d-stabbed-wife-after-she-insults-him)}

## â€œmurder to manslaughter ğŸ¡ª provocation must be calculated to inflame passion of an objective reasonable person. Suddenness required. Words alone not sufficient.â€ {#â€œmurder-to-manslaughter-ğŸ¡ª-provocation-must-be-calculated-to-inflame-passion-of-an-objective-reasonable-person.-suddenness-required.-words-alone-not-sufficient.â€}

**Rule**:	For provocation to be adequate to mitigate murder to manslaughter, the provocation must be calculated to inflame the passion of a reasonable person and tend to cause him to act for the moment from passion rather than reason.

* **Only a few specific circumstances (e.g., battery, sudden mutual combat) can serve as legally adequate provocation**  
    
  **Facts**:	D and his wife got into an argument. She told him she wanted a divorce and asked, â€œWhat are you going to do \[about it\]?â€ He grabbed a knife hidden behind a pillow and stabbed her **19** times. 

**Holding/Rationale:** The victim did not provoke the defendant. **Murder not manslaughter.** 

* Words alone are inadequate to constitute provocation.   
* D couldnâ€™t have feared for bodily harm because his wife was much smaller than him.


**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Defendant argument**  
  * No mens rea; rather provoked in heat of moment w/ mental disturbance  
  * Asking for court to include new category  
* **State argument**  
  * Mens rea; malice is present  
* **2 options:**   
  * **Extreme objective standard** (Ask whether an objective reasonable person would have been enraged to the point of murder in the same circumstances) **vs.**   
  * **Subjective standard:** was this specific defendant subjectively in the heat of passion/out of his mind â€“ case by case  
    * In this case, they use an objective standard, finding that provocation by words is not sufficient to justify heat-of-the-moment killing â€“ it was murder not manslaughter. No new category.  
* Remember biases ğŸ¡ª court assumes that D couldnâ€™t have truly feared bodily harm because wife is smaller than him. Gender roles/patriarchal underpinnings in case.   
* **Distinction with *Carroll*** *\=* here, in heat of argument; Carroll was after argument ended so premeditated/1st\-degree

## Maher v. People (Mich., 1862\) â€“ MINORITY \- Provocation \= jury q (D shoots wifeâ€™s lover in saloon) {#maher-v.-people-(mich.,-1862)-â€“-minority---provocation-=-jury-q-(d-shoots-wifeâ€™s-lover-in-saloon)}

## â€œmurder to manslaughter ğŸ¡ª if intentional killing committed in HOP is caused by reasonable provocation before reasonable time has passed for HOP to coolâ€ {#â€œmurder-to-manslaughter-ğŸ¡ª-if-intentional-killing-committed-in-hop-is-caused-by-reasonable-provocation-before-reasonable-time-has-passed-for-hop-to-coolâ€}

**Rule**:	A criminal charge of murder may be reduced to manslaughter if the intentional killing is committed in the heat of passion produced by a reasonable provocation before a reasonable time has lapsed for the passion to cool.

* **Provoking circumstances need not conform to any pre-established categories and that it is normally a question for the jury to decide whether the facts as a whole demonstrate sufficient provocation**

  **Facts**:	D suspected his wife was cheating on him. He followed her and her lover and saw that they disappeared into the woods. D shot the lover, but didnâ€™t kill him. He was charged with assault with the intent to murder. 

**Holding/Rationale:** Evidence of provocation shouldâ€™ve been admitted at the trial level.

* It wouldâ€™ve been within the juryâ€™s competence to find whether the conduct was committed as a result of provocation.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Significance:** Softens the â€œwords are not enough ruleâ€ in *Girouard* and holds that provocation is a jury question.  
* **HARCOURT:** This is an arcane understand of provocation motivated by a patriarchal conception of a manâ€™s â€œrightâ€ to his woman. Look at the year this case was decided.   
* This case follows a more subjective standard â€“ provoking circumstances donâ€™t need to fit in pre-established categories, look at what the specific individual was going through.   
  * Even though the standard was of a reasonable man, jury instruction was specific to D and what a reasonable man in *these specific circumstances* would do.    
* **Defendant argument**  
  * No mens rea; provocation; heat of passion  
* **State argument**   
  * Mens rea present; intent to kill

## People v. Casassa (NY Ct APP., 1980\) â€“ MPC approach: EED (subj.) \+ Reasonable excuse (Obj) (Ex refused gifts; stabbed her {#people-v.-casassa-(ny-ct-app.,-1980)-â€“-mpc-approach:-eed-(subj.)-+-reasonable-excuse-(obj)-(ex-refused-gifts;-stabbed-her}

## â€œmurder to manslaughter ğŸ¡ª EED \+ reasonable explanation/excuse for actions from an subj. \+ obj. analysis (NY)â€ {#â€œmurder-to-manslaughter-ğŸ¡ª-eed-+-reasonable-explanation/excuse-for-actions-from-an-subj.-+-obj.-analysis-(ny)â€}

**Rule**:	A NY defendant may reduce a charge of murder to manslaughter if he is able to show â€œextreme emotional disturbanceâ€ and that there was a reasonable explanation or excuse for his actions as determined by the court from both a subjective and objective analysis.

* Reasonableness from viewpoint of a person in the Dâ€™s situation under the circumstances as he believes them to be  
* **Provocation is â€œextreme mental or emotional disturbanceâ€**

  **Facts**:	Dâ€™s girlfriend broke up with him, and he began stalking her. On the night of the killing, he broke into her home to offer her gifts. When she refused them, he stabbed her several times. 

**Statute:** Under NY law, it is an affirmative defense to second degree murder that the defendant acted under extreme   
emotional distress (EED) for which there is a reasonable explanation/excuse.

* Dâ€™s burden of proof

**Holding/Rationale:** Dâ€™s E(ED) doesnâ€™t have a reasonable explanation/excuse

* **Subjective:** Yes, the D was clearly emotionally distressed.  
* **Objective(ish):** His emotional distress was not reasonably justified from the viewpoint of a person in the defendantâ€™s circumstances. 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* MPC approach is a test that asks whether the defendant acted â€œunder the influence of EED for which there is a reasonable explanation or excuse. The Code then directs that the determination of the reasonableness of the explanation or excuse shall be made from the viewpoint of a person in the actorâ€™s situation under the circumstances as he believes them to beâ€¦designedly ambiguous (458-59)  
* **Corine CRITIQUE:** case allows certain facts to matter and others not  
  * What is the role of criminal law in a political context thatâ€™s filled with bias and prejudice?   
    * To correct â€“ this would lead me to disagree with MPC because you canâ€™t correct something thatâ€™s filled with bias using a standard thatâ€™s biased towards the D.    
    * Or to reflect? Then adopting MPC makes more sense 

# Felony Murder {#felony-murder}

**Can apply to one person â€“ there doesnâ€™t need to be a co-felon. Can also apply to manslaughter.**

![][image6]

| Common Law Felony Murder Felony Murder: killing that is a direct result of an *intentional commission of a felony* will establish felony murder and is *not limited by foreseeability,* or lack thereof Mens rea \= specific intent for felony \+ NONE for murder intent to commit felony accounts for the mens rea that resulted in the death of victim |
| :---- |
| **\*strict liability** that eliminates mens rea requirement for murder (*actus reus \+ causation still required*) \*BUT felony murder requires **specific intent for the felony** (*purposely or knowingly*). If felony committed negligently or recklessly, hard to find felony murder.  ğŸ¡ªIn other words, lack of mens rea requirement for the murder, ratchets up mens rea requirement for felony |

| MPC 210.2(b) Felony Murder MPC has no standalone felony-murder provision. (1) â€¦Criminal homicide constitutes \[felony\] murder when: (b) it is committed recklessly under the circumstances manifesting extreme indifference to the value of human lifeâ€¦ Felony-Murder: â€¦\[extreme\] recklessness and indifference are presumed if the actor is engaged or is an accomplice in the commission of, or an attempt to commit, or flight after committing or attempting to commit robbery, rape or deviate sexual intercourse by force or threat of force, arson, burglary, kidnapping or felonious escape. |
| :---- |
| **\*rebuttable presumption** of recklessness (a conscious appreciation and disregard as to risk of death), NOT strict liability like common law. **Contrast with common law. ** \*D can rebut presumption w/lack of foreseeability or evidence indicating no recklessness \*once D rebuts presumption ğŸ¡ª **burden shifts to State** to prove manslaughter/negligent homicide took place \* The MPC doesn't like the felony murder rule, but most states nonetheless retain some form of the rule. |

## Limitations on Felony Murder {#limitations-on-felony-murder}

**Agency theory (Majority)**: Imposes liability only when the killing is by the felon or one of his agents. Focus is on the actor. No liability if shooting is done by a victim or a police officer. (*Canola*) **Focused on identity of killer.** 

* **POLICY**  
  * **Who is the killer?**  
    * **Retribution:** based on identity of killer so more closely tied to moral culpability \+ non-felons are justified in any acts of violence they do (self- defense) so no moral culpability to transfer to D  
    * BUT is there a Foucaultâ€™s Birth of Prison problem here? Punishing people based on who they are, rather than what they do? Probably not, because it also punishes based on what people do

    

**Proximate cause theory (Minority)**: Liability attaches for any death proximately resulting from the unlawful activity, though it is most often applied in those states that follow the enumerated felonies that are inherently dangerous to life approach to felony murder. I.e., foreseeable that death could result from felonies that involve weapon. **Focused on** **foreseeability.** 

* More expansive liability, likely to include everything.

* BUT also affirmative defense for felons who can show they had no reason to anticipate use of deadly force.

* POLICY

  * Was death a foreseeable consequence of committing the felony?

    * **Retribution**: we want to punish people for all acts that they reasonably foresee could occur from their criminal behavior

    * **Utilitarian**: low deterrence value in punishing people for all reasonably foreseeable acts b/c unlikely that D actually considers â€œreasonably foreseeableâ€ consequences, which is very broad


| AGENCY THEORY Identity of killer | Target	of	felony victim | Innocent	bystander victim | Co-D victim |
| :---- | :---- | :---- | :---- |
| **D kills** | Y | Y | Y  |
| **Co-D kills** *though it depends on the agreement between D and Co-D (e.g. in on robbery but not in on anything else*) \= *limited agency* | Y | Y | Y |
| **Target of felony kills** | N | N | N |
| **Police kills** | N | N | N |
| **Innocent	bystander kills** | N | N | N |

  \*No liability when murder is done by victim/police officer.


  **NY** uses **proximate cause theory**. Felons responsible for every death except that of a co-felon. (contrast with *Canola*)

  **NYPL 125.25**: Person is guilty of murder if, â€œin the course of and in furtherance of \[designated felonies\], he, or another participantâ€¦causes the death of a person other than one of the participants.â€


| PROXIMATE CAUSE THEORY foreseeability | Target	of	felony victim | Innocent	bystander victim | Co-D victim |
| :---- | :---- | :---- | :---- |
| **D kills** | Y | Y | N |
| **Co-D kills** *though it depends on the agreement between D and Co-D (e.g. in on robbery but not in on anything else*) \= *limited agency* | Y | Y | N |
| **Target of felony kills** | Y | Y | N |
| **Police kills** | Y | Y | N |
| **Innocent bystander kills** | Y | Y | N |

  \*Proximate cause theory has more expansive liability than agency theory and likely to catch everything.

  \*BUT also affirmative defense for felons who can show they had no reason to anticipate use of deadly force.


  N.B. some circumstances (ie. â€œimplied maliceâ€ or â€œdepraved heart murderâ€) where conscious disregard for life will hold D liable for murders committed by non- felons.

**Dangerous Felony Requirement**: Felony murder rule is limited to felonies that are dangerous and likely to cause death. (*Serne*) 

**â€œIn furtherance of the felonyâ€ Requirement**: Felony murder only applies when the act of killing is done in furtherance of the felony.

* Requires closeness in time & distance  
* Causation: but-for and proximate

See:

* *Stamp*: Felon is strictly liable for any deaths that happened during the felony even without proximate cause (actual cause is enough)  
  * **Note:** Under *Serne*, accidental killing like this case would NOT be a felony murder  
  * Args. against strict liability: Punishment of death for both robbery and homicide so perverse incentive to kill witnesses when committing lesser crime of robbery.  
    * Utilitarian: If you canâ€™t foresee activity (no PC), canâ€™t be deterred.

## Regina v. SernÃ© (UK., 1887\) â€“ act must be dangerous and likely to cause death (Arson insurance scheme) {#regina-v.-sernÃ©-(uk.,-1887)-â€“-act-must-be-dangerous-and-likely-to-cause-death-(arson-insurance-scheme)}

## â€œintent to commit felonious act known to be dangerous to life \+ likely to cause anotherâ€™s death \= felony murderâ€ {#â€œintent-to-commit-felonious-act-known-to-be-dangerous-to-life-+-likely-to-cause-anotherâ€™s-death-=-felony-murderâ€}

**Rule**:	When one does an act known to be dangerous to life, for the purpose of committing a felony, the act is chargeable as murder. 

* **Narrowed** **felony murder** to include only homicides caused by an action that is (1) dangerous in and of itself, and (2) likely to result in the death of another, and (3) taken by a defendant with the intent to commit a felony.   
  * **NOTE:** The act must be dangerous **and** likely to cause death. There are many instances where you might commit a felony in a non-dangerous/non-risky manner and still kill a person. Those donâ€™t count as felony-murder. 

  **Facts**:	D and his family were in severe debt, living in a house above a store. D took out a life insurance policy on his son (an â€œimbecileâ€) and insured the store inventory, furniture and rent. A month after policy was taken out, store and house burned down, killing his two sons. D and friend were allegedly seen together in store shortly before fire started. Alleged that they willfully set fire and were indicted for insured sonâ€™s murder.

**Holding/Rationale:** Only an action taken with the intent to commit a felony that is known to create a danger to human life can constitute felony murder. 

## People v. Stamp (Cal., 1862\) â€“ strict liability for resulting deaths; not limited by forseeability (heart attack during robbery) {#people-v.-stamp-(cal.,-1862)-â€“-strict-liability-for-resulting-deaths;-not-limited-by-forseeability-(heart-attack-during-robbery)}

## â€œif homicide \= direct causal result of felony, the felony murder rule applies (doesnâ€™t need to be likely COD)â€ {#â€œif-homicide-=-direct-causal-result-of-felony,-the-felony-murder-rule-applies-(doesnâ€™t-need-to-be-likely-cod)â€}

**Rule**:	A defendant may be convicted of murder if, during the attempt or actual commission of an inherently dangerous crime, a killing occurs. **Strict liability.** 

* View generally held in American courts: felony murder rule imposes strict liability for killings that result from the commission of a felony, regardless of whether the felons knew or should have known that their conduct was endangering life (i.e. dispensed with the mens rea requirement but still needs actus reus and causation).  
* **NOTE**: This seems to go against *SernÃ©â€™s* rule that the act must be likely to cause death. Simply pointing a gun, without pulling the trigger, might fall closer to *SernÃ©â€™s* robbery example.  
    
  **Facts**:	 D burglarized manâ€™s store, robbed him at gunpoint. After robbery, man (60-year-old with history of heart disease) died of a heart attack. Doctor testified that heart attack was induced by fright.  

**Holding/Rationale:** First-degree murder conviction is upheld. 

* Felony-murder doctrine not limited to foreseeable deaths; a felon is instead held **strictly liable** for all killing committed by him/his accomplices in the course of the felony  
* As long as the homicide is the direct causal result of the robbery the felony-murder rule applies whether or not the death was a natural/probable consequence of the robbery.  
* The robber takes his victim as he finds him. 

## State v. Canola (NJ., 1977\) â€“ MAJ: Agency Theory \- death of co-felon Limitation (Jewelry store robbery) {#state-v.-canola-(nj.,-1977)-â€“-maj:-agency-theory---death-of-co-felon-limitation-(jewelry-store-robbery)}

## â€œfelony murder rule canâ€™t be applied to D when death of a co-felon results from resisting victimâ€ {#â€œfelony-murder-rule-canâ€™t-be-applied-to-d-when-death-of-a-co-felon-results-from-resisting-victimâ€}

**Rule**:	The felony murder rule does not extend to render a felon liable for the death of a co-felon resulting from the actions of a resisting victim.  

**Facts**:	Jewelry store owner resisted an attempted armed robbery by D and Dâ€™s accomplices. One accomplice and store owner exchanged fire, both died. D and two accomplices indicted on one count of robbery and two counts of murder (store owner and co-felon). D found guilty on both murder counts and sentenced to 2 concurrent terms of life imprisonment. 

**Holding/Rationale:** No, under NJ law, a D cannot be found guilty of the killing of an accomplice by the armed robbery victim. 

* Court subscribes to **agency theory** (majority rule)  
  * Court doesnâ€™t want to expand felony murder rule; it enacted at a time when all killing were punishable by DP, which is no longer the case.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Agency v. proximate cause theory  
  * **Agency theory:** only if the act of killing is done by a co-felon or someone acting in concert with a co- felon will the felony-murder rule apply   
  * **Proximate-cause theory:** the central issue is whether the killing (regardless of who did it) is within the foreseeable risk in committing the felony. Minority. 





# Unintended Killings {#unintended-killings}

**Common Law Elements**  
Accused does not intend to kill her victim, but malice is implied because the defendantâ€™s conduct demonstrates extreme recklessness or indifference to human life.

**MPC Â§ 210.2(b): Unintended Murder**   
Unintended killing is murder when it is (1) committed recklessly as per 2.02(2)(c)) and (2) and â€œunder circumstances manifesting extreme indifference to the value of human life.â€ 

* **NOTE**: Under the MPC, what is known as depraved heart murder at common law is collapsed under what the MPC defines as â€œreckless murder,â€ which requires a mens rea of recklessness

## Manslaughter v Murder: Extent of Recklnesses {#manslaughter-v-murder:-extent-of-recklnesses}

**Circumstances Under Which an Unintended Killing Constituted Murder Instead of Manslaughter: Extent of Recklessness**  

* Unintended killing with ordinary recklessness amounts to manslaughter, but if combined with â€œextreme indifferenceâ€ may constitute murder.    
* How to draw the line between murder and manslaughter?  
  * Risk: objective level  
  * Awareness: Dâ€™s subjective awareness of risk  
  * Standard of care: amount of care D exercised   
* Manslaughter (recklessness) \= high risk, was aware, gross deviation from standard of care  
* Murder (extreme recklessness) \= even higher risk, more aware, even grosser deviation from standard of care   
  * **Note: donâ€™t forget an omission can be extremely reckless to warrant 2nd\-degree murder\!**

| CL Murder | â†” Distinguished based on how wicked and depraved the killing seemed to be  | CL Manslaughter  | MPC Â§210.2 |
| ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
|     Causing the death of another **with malice aforethought**     Intent to kill     Intent to inflict significant bodily harm **â€œDepraved heart murderâ€**/extreme recklessness & indifference to life Felony murder  |  |     Causing the death of another without malice aforethought     Voluntary: heat of passion     Voluntary: self-defense     Involuntary: reckless     Involuntary: criminal negligence  | Treats an unintended killing as murder when it is 1\) committed â€œrecklesslyâ€ (â€œconscious disregards a substantial and unjustifiable riskâ€ resulting from his conduct and â€œits disregard involves a gross deviation from the standard of conduct that a law-abiding person would observe in the actorâ€™s situationâ€) and 2\) Â â€œunder circumstances manifesting extreme indifference to the value of human life.â€  |

## Commonwealth v. Malone (PA., 1946\) â€“ Depraved Heart at CL (Russian roulette) {#commonwealth-v.-malone-(pa.,-1946)-â€“-depraved-heart-at-cl-(russian-roulette)}

## â€œmalice present when D commits a crime of gross recklessness that they reasonable anticipate will likely result in death of anotherâ€ {#â€œmalice-present-when-d-commits-a-crime-of-gross-recklessness-that-they-reasonable-anticipate-will-likely-result-in-death-of-anotherâ€}

**Rule**:	When an individual commits a crime of gross recklessness for which he must reasonably anticipate that death to another is *likely* to result, he exhibits that â€œwickedness of disposition, hardness of heart, cruelty, recklessness of consequences, and a mind regardless of social dutyâ€ which proves that malice was present. 

**Facts**:	 17-year old killed by 13-year old friend while playing Russian Roulette. 

**Holding/Rationale:** Murder charge is correct. 

* The killing was result of an act intentionally done by D, in reckless and wanton disregard of the consequences. Therefore, the killing was murder, for malice is evidenced by the intentional doing of an uncalled-for act in callous disregard of its likely effect on others.  
* Lack of motive is not dispositive. 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* What is the real possibility? **Harcourt** think either way the court didnâ€™t get it right.  
* Should D have known that the risk is so great? (c.f. strict liability in torts) Courtâ€™s reasoning would push us from reckless manslaughter to murder. The key issue here is that it does not seem itâ€™s intentional. Unlike heat of passion, which converts intentional to unintentional, here the doctrine converts unintentional to intentional (at least in terms of culpability).

## Involuntary Manslaughter vs. Negligent Homicide: Awareness {#involuntary-manslaughter-vs.-negligent-homicide:-awareness}

MPC created extra category of negligent homicide b/c shouldnâ€™t be punished for same degree as recklessness when youâ€™re unaware.

1) Was D aware or unaware of risk?  
   1. Common law â€“ doesnâ€™t matter b/c no negligent homicide category ğŸ¡ª both risks that a reasonable person would be aware of and D was aware of fall under involuntary manslaughter  
   2. MPC â€“ aware ğŸ¡ª reckless homicide (involuntary or voluntary manslaughter); unaware Ã  negligent homicide  
      1. **Main Q:** Was Ds lack of awareness **civil or criminal negligence**?  
         1. Use Harcourtâ€™s 3-D graph to assess **risk, awareness, standard of care**  
2) **MPC:** involuntary manslaughter \= **subjective.** Requires D to have been subjectively aware of risk *(People v. Hall â€“ skiing collision)*  
3) **Common law:** involuntary manslaughter \= **subjective \+ objective** b/c no negligent homicide category *(Commonwealth v. Welansky â€“ fire in club)*

### Common Law Approach: Only involuntary manslaughter

**Involuntary Manslaughter** 

* **Elements at Common Law:** intentional failure \[the act\] (2) that causes (3) death of (4) another.   
* **Mens rea:** wantonly (CL) or recklessly (MPC).  
  * Wanton/reckless conduct \= intentional conduct, either by commission or omission, that has a high likelihood that substantial harm will result.  
* Often involves an omission for which D had a legal duty to act.   
* Notes:  
  * Killing is not intentional   
    * Example: In a simple fight, you punch someone and they die. You couldnâ€™t have thought that one punch could kill the victim. You lack the intent to kill, but you did kill so ğŸ¡ª involuntary manslaughter.   
    * Egg shell P rule does not apply. THIS IS NOT TORTS  
  * If you kill someone during a non-dangerous felony, it might be involuntary manslaughter 

| Civil vs. Criminal Negligence: *Williams* |  |
| :---- | :---- |
| **Civil Negligence** | **Criminal Negligence/Liability**  |
| Negligence or gross negligence  | Wanton or reckless conduct where grave danger to others must have been apparent and D must have chosen to run the risk rather than alter his conduct so as to avoid the act or omission which caused the harm  If D chooses to run the risk, it is wanton or reckless conduct no matter whether the ordinary man would have realized the gravity of the danger or not  |

## Commonwealth v. Welansky (MA., 1944\) â€“ CL Involuntary Manslaughter; OBJ or Subj. (nightclub fire) {#commonwealth-v.-welansky-(ma.,-1944)-â€“-cl-involuntary-manslaughter;-obj-or-subj.-(nightclub-fire)}

## â€œmanslaughter can be based on omissions and affirmative actsâ€ {#â€œmanslaughter-can-be-based-on-omissions-and-affirmative-actsâ€}

**Rule**:	The conviction of manslaughter may be based on omissions as well as on affirmative acts. Wanton or reckless conduct which results in homicide are what constitute involuntary manslaughter. 

* Usually wanton or reckless conduct consists of an affirmative act, particularly if thereâ€™s a duty of care.  
* **Mens rea** â€“ recklessness  
    
  **Facts**:	D, owner of a nightclub, charged with involuntary manslaughter for death of 100 patrons at his club resulting from a fire. As owner of club, D had a legal duty to his invitees to use reasonable care in keeping the premises safe for their use and preventing (un)known dangers.

**Statute:** Involuntary manslaughter requires wanton or reckless conduct.

**Holding/Rationale:** Involuntary manslaughter conviction upheld.

* (1) D was aware that venue was not in compliance with local safety regulation; and  
* (2) D knew that construction plans for new party of lounge precluded the possibility of installing the doors necessary to comply with safety regulations 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Wanton/reckless conduct \= intentional conduct, by commission or omission, that has a high likelihood of substantial harm  
  * Goes beyond civil negligence   
* **Coconut Grove** \- CL conduct doesnâ€™t become criminal until it passes from negligence to wanton or reckless. (MA doesnâ€™t have CL criminal negligence.)

### MPC APPROACH: Involuntary Mans. or negligent Homicide

**Main Q:** Whether *lack of awareness* was merely **civil negligence** (deviation from standard of care) or **criminal negligence** (gross deviation)?

* Use Harcourtâ€™s 3-D graph to determine whether civil or criminal  
  * i. Risk  
  * ii. Awareness  
  * iii. Standard of care 

![][image7]

| Â§210.3 Involuntary Manslaughter \*criminal negligence (gross deviation); exception in *Williams* prior to change in WA law  | â†” Distinguished by whether D was aware of the unwarranted risk he was creating (â€œis aware ofâ€ vs. â€œshould have knownâ€) | Â§210.4 Negligent Homicide (= involuntary manslaughter at common law)   \*civil negligence â€“ deviation from SOC |
| :---- | ----- | :---- |
| Killing is manslaughter if actor was reckless  Consider: Awareness Amount of danger How much care D was exercising Value of the activity itself   Actor is aware of the substantial risk, but ignores it anyway |  | Killing is negligent homicide if actor should have been aware of such a risk  Actor isnâ€™t aware of the substantial risk, even though he should have been  |

**Difference btw Involuntary Manslaughter (MPC 210.3) and Negligent Homicide (MPC 210.4)**

* **Distinguished by whether the defendant was aware of the unwarranted risk he was creating.**  
* Manslaughter if actor was (a) â€œrecklessâ€ (see 2.02) OR (b) murder \+ extreme mental or emotional disturbance for which here is a reasonable explanation/excuse. **Felony of 3rd degree.**   
  * (a) Recklessness â€“ is the defendant â€œconsciously disregarded a substantial and unjustifiable risk that his conduct would cause the death of anotherâ€ and if the risk was â€œof such a nature and degree that, considering the nature and purpose of his conduct and the circumstances known to him, its disregard involves a gross deviation from the standard of conduct that a law-abiding person would observe in the actorâ€™s situation.â€  
  * (b) Reasonableness from perspective of a person in the defendantâ€™s situation under the circumstances as he believes them to be  
    * VERY SUBJECTIVE STANDARD, which can be more defendant friendly.   
* Negligent homicide is when a person *should have been* aware of such a risk (negligence). **Felony of 3rd degree.**   
* Elevated to murder if â€œmanifesting extreme indifference to human life.â€

**Policy Rationale for MPCâ€™s extra category of negligent homicide:**

* **Retributivist:** No one should be convicted of an offense as serious as manslaughter in absence of subjective fault (conscious disregard)

NO contributory negligence defense for criminal liability, but Vs conduct may play into whether Ds conduct was proximate 

## People v. Hall (CO., 2000\) â€“ Involuntary manslaughter under MPC. Subj. (skiing collision) {#people-v.-hall-(co.,-2000)-â€“-involuntary-manslaughter-under-mpc.-subj.-(skiing-collision)}

## â€œgross deviation from SOC \+ creation of substantial and unjustifiable risk \+ death \= reckless manslaughterâ€ {#â€œgross-deviation-from-soc-+-creation-of-substantial-and-unjustifiable-risk-+-death-=-reckless-manslaughterâ€}

**Rule**:	When a personâ€™s conduct constitutes a gross deviation from the standard of care and creates a substantial and unjustifiable risk of death to another, consciously disregards that risk and death subsequently results, the person may be charged with felony reckless manslaughter. 

**Facts**:	While skiing, D collided with victim who was on the slope below. Victim died as a result. D was charged with reckless manslaughter. 

**Statute:** Reckless manslaughter requires that a person â€œrecklessly cause the death of another person.â€

* Statute comes from MPC definition of reckless mens rea at Â§2.02. Conduct is reckless when the actor consciously disregards a substantial and unjustifiable risk that death may result. 

**Holding/Rationale:** A risk of death that has less than a 50% chance of occurring can still be substantial if the circumstances warrant it. 

* Here, Dâ€™s excessive speed, lack of control and improper technique for skiing bumps significantly increased the risk.  The deviation is unjustified just by personal enjoyment.   
* A statute stipulates a skierâ€™s duty not to injure others (the standard of care). Dâ€™ s knowledge and training could give rise to inference that he is aware of the risk.   
* Therefore, a reasonable person could have concluded that D created a substantial, unjustifiable risk. \[Note how the court considers all the elements.\]

## State v. Williams (WA., 1971\) â€“ Outlier: Navigating between civil and Criminal negligence â€“ (NA baby death) {#state-v.-williams-(wa.,-1971)-â€“-outlier:-navigating-between-civil-and-criminal-negligence-â€“-(na-baby-death)}

## â€œBAD LAW: ordinary (civil) negligence \+ proximate cause \= involuntary manslaughterâ€ {#â€œbad-law:-ordinary-(civil)-negligence-+-proximate-cause-=-involuntary-manslaughterâ€}

**Rule**:	If a person commits ordinary negligence, i.e. fails to exercise ordinary caution that a reasonable person would exercise under the same or similar circumstances, and such negligence proximately causes the death of another, a person is guilty of involuntary manslaughter.

* **WA later amended the statute to require criminal negligence**


  **Facts**:	Native American mother and stepfather convicted of manslaughter for negligently failing to take their baby to a doctor for abscessed tooth that eventually led to death.  

**Statute:** In WA, involuntary manslaughter is committed if the death of the victim is the proximate result of   
simple/ordinary/civil negligence. 

**Holding/Rationale:** The failure so to do in this case is ordinary or simple negligence, and such negligence is sufficient to support a conviction of statutory manslaughter.

* Defendants were sufficiently put on notice concerning the symptoms of the babyâ€™s illness and lack of improvement in the babyâ€™s apparent condition in the period from September 1 to September 5, 1968 to have required them to have obtained medical care for the child.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Significance:** This case is navigating the space between civil liability and criminal negligence.   
  * Ds guilty of involuntary manslaughter without subjective awareness of how sick baby was b/c they did not act as a reasonable person would have in the same situation (bring baby to doctor). **Note:** WA code defined manslaughter as simple tort negligence (diff. from MPC and common law, which both require **gross negligence**).  
    * **Broad vs. narrow time framing:**  
      * Broad \= take into account Ds socioeconomic condition \+ limited education \+ fear welfare department would take their child away based on their cousinâ€™s experience.  
      * Narrow \= Child gets sick and parents noticed baby had a toothache 2 weeks prior to death.  
  * **Harcourt:** Havenâ€™t parents been punished enough? At what point is point of no return? After day 5, treatment wouldnâ€™t have mattered so parents not getting treatment for 2 weeks seems less condemning.  
  * **POLICY:** \*Native American parents are being punished for who they are (socioeconomic condition \+ limited education). Foucaultâ€™s Birth of Prison.  
* Manslaughter statutes involved in *Williams* were repealed in 1975\. The current statutes create 2 degrees of manslaughter: recklessly causing death and causing death by criminal (not ordinary, as happened here) negligence. In accord w/ generally prevailing law, Washington no longer imposes liability w/ cases involving ordinary negligence. 

# Causation {#causation}

Criminal liability only attaches where there is both **factual cause (â€œbut forâ€)** and **proximate (legal) cause**.

* **Only relevant in result crimes**  
* Usually arises in homicide cases where: 1\) intended death occurs in a way not intended, or 2\) where unintended death occurs in an unlikely way   
* MPC tracks common law for causation.

**MPC Â§ 2.03(1)**  
Conduct is the cause of a result when:

* (1) it is an antecedent but for which the result in question would not have occurred;  
* (2) the relationship between the conduct and result satisfies any additional causal requirements imposed by the Code or by the law defining the offense.  
  * The MPC automatically requires factual or â€œbut forâ€ cause.  
  * Proximate cause comes into play according to the statute or jurisdiction.

**POLICY:** Function of causation requirement:

* **Utilitarian:** determines responsibility for resulting harm among people/forces. Can only deter if D actually caused Vs harm  
* **Retributive:** Links punishment to personal responsibility for crimes. D should only be punished if D caused Vs harm 

| Burden: The prosecutor must prove the causal link (factual and proximate cause) beyond a reasonable doubt.  |  |  |
| :---- | :---- | :---- |
| **Factual Cause â€œBut forâ€** | **Proximate Cause**  | **Intervening Cause** |
| Aka the harm would not have occurred in the absence of Dâ€™s act  **Concurrent causes** still satisfy cause in fact b/c they are substantial factors  | In addition to being the but for cause, the act must bear a sufficiently close relationship to the resulting harm  **Direct causes** are always proximate causes Must be foreseeable (*Acosta*) Largely a question of public policy and value judgments on Ds moral culpability  | Breaks the chain of causation, relieving D of criminal responsibility  Whether an intervening cause supersedes Dâ€™s criminal responsibility turns on: 1\) how substantial the contribution to social harm was and 2\) how foreseeable the intervening cause was In general, **D will still be liable** for intervening causes if they are of the same type of harm, only injure a different person/property, or harm that occurs is less than the one intended. Unforeseeable manner **D will NOT be liable** and intervening cause will be superseding if superseding cause was unforeseeable or substantial. Last actor Grossly negligent  |

## People v. Acosta (CA., 1991\) â€“ forseeability in proximate cause \- outer limit case (high speed chase) {#people-v.-acosta-(ca.,-1991)-â€“-forseeability-in-proximate-cause---outer-limit-case-(high-speed-chase)}

## â€œproximate cause can still be found in intervening causes if result from Dâ€™s conduct is reasonably foreseeableâ€ {#â€œproximate-cause-can-still-be-found-in-intervening-causes-if-result-from-dâ€™s-conduct-is-reasonably-foreseeableâ€}

**Rule**:	Proximate cause can still be found in case of intervening acts if those acts are not highly extraordinary and reasonably foreseeable to result from Ds conduct.

* Proximate cause must be foreseeable. The standard is to exclude extraordinary results, and allow the trier of fact to determine the issue on the particular facts of the case using â€œthe common sense of the common man as to common things.â€  
* **NOTE:** Ds who create peril to human life will be held responsible even if another person, including the victim, a would-be rescuer, or a police officer, actually brings about the harm.


  **Facts**:	Due to Dâ€™s fleeing from police, a high-speed chase ensued, which resulted in the collision of 2 helicopters and the deaths of three occupants of one of the helicopters. A retired FAA investigator testified that the pilots violated several FAA regulations..


  **Holding/Rationale:** Acostaâ€™s conduct was the proximate cause of the deaths.

* But for Acostaâ€™s conduct of fleeing the police, the helicopters would never have been in a position to crash. The deaths were not highly extraordinary and reasonably might have been contemplated.  
  * A foreseeability inquiry considers whether or not the resultâ€”here, deathâ€”was highly extraordinary.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Very broad interpretation of foreseeability (**outer limits**) Includes reasonable anticipation of intervening negligence by others.  
  * Although a two-helicopter midair collision has never been reported, it was a â€œpossible consequence which reasonably might have been contemplated.â€ Acosta was found liable because it was â€œwithin the realm of likelihoodâ€ to occur and he could have foreseen it.   
* Reflects the idea that defendants who create peril to human life should not be surprised if harm occurs as a result of what they did.   
* **POLICY CONSIDERATIONS**  
  * **Retributivist:** intervening act of negligence is about blameworthiness  
  * **Utilitarian:** probability of this particular accident is very low; no utility in holding D liable b/c low deterrence value

## People v. Arzon (NY., 1978\) â€“ concurrent causation for PC Okay (2 fires) {#people-v.-arzon-(ny.,-1978)-â€“-concurrent-causation-for-pc-okay-(2-fires)}

## â€œintervening harm that is ultimate COD doesnâ€™t excuse D from liability if the deadly harm and Dâ€™s actions are foreseeable and reasonably relatedâ€ {#â€œintervening-harm-that-is-ultimate-cod-doesnâ€™t-excuse-d-from-liability-if-the-deadly-harm-and-dâ€™s-actions-are-foreseeable-and-reasonably-relatedâ€}

**Rule**:	An intervening harm that is the ultimate cause of death does not excuse D from culpability provided Dâ€™s actions are (1) a sufficiently direct cause of the death and (2) D should have foreseen the harm as reasonably related to his actions.

**Facts**:	Defendant started a fire on the fifth floor of an abandoned building to keep warm, and firefighters responded to fight the fire. Meanwhile, another fire started independently on the second floor trapped the firefighters. Overcome by smoke from the first and second fires, one firefighter sustained life-taking injuries.

**Holding/Rationale:** The fire set by Arzon was an indispensable link in the chain of events that resulted in the firemanâ€™s death. Arzonâ€™s acts placed the fireman in a position where he was particularly vulnerable to the separate and independent force, the fire on the second floor.

* In all likelihood, the firefighter wouldnâ€™t have died had someone else not set the second-story fire. Nonetheless, Arzonâ€™s fire satisfies the â€œbut-forâ€ requirement.  
  * If the defendant had not set the chain of events in motion, the firefighter would not have died.   
  * Moreover, the court can point to Arzonâ€™s conduct â€“ starting the fire â€“ as one component of the forces that caused the firefighterâ€™s death.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Cause in Fact:** But for Ds arson, V would not have been in location to be harmed by second fire.  
* **PC**: Foreseeable that firemen would respond to fire D started and be put in danger of their lives.  
* **NOTE:** It is not necessary that the ultimate harm be directly intended by the actor, but the harm should be foreseen as being related to the acts of the defendant.   
  * A mere probable connection between Dâ€™s conduct and another personâ€™s death is not sufficient to support a charge of homicide

## Suicide  {#suicide}

**Current Suicide Law**

* One who successfully urges or assists another to commit suicide is not guilty of murder, at least so long as deceased was mentally responsible and was not forced or deceived.  
* Most states also reject possibility of manslaughter or negligent homicide conviction, provided that deceasedâ€™s actions were fully voluntary.  
* **MPC Â§210.5(1):** *Causing Suicide as Criminal Homicide.* Permits convicting a person of criminal homicide for causing another to take his life, but only if he purposely causes such suicide by force, duress or deception. Â   
  * (2) *Aiding or Soliciting Suicide as an Independent Offense.* Conduct causes suicide or attempted suicide \+ some other misdemeanor

**Takeaways:** *Kevorkian* and *Campbell* give you both (major) elements for murder

* *Campbell*: Hope for a result â‰  intending a result (mens rea)  
* *Kevorkian*: Must participate in the final overt act that causes death (conduct/causing a result)

## People v. Campbell (MI., 1983\) â€“ suicide excluded from def. of homicide (suicide encouraged) {#people-v.-campbell-(mi.,-1983)-â€“-suicide-excluded-from-def.-of-homicide-(suicide-encouraged)}
