## â€œsuicide  homicide; encouraging someone to commit suicide, even after providing the weapon, is not intentâ€ {#â€œsuicide-homicide;-encouraging-someone-to-commit-suicide,-even-after-providing-the-weapon,-is-not-intentâ€}

**Rule**:	Homicide is the killing of one human being by another and excludes the act of one person providing a weapon to another person who ultimately commits suicide.

* **Takeaway:** (1) Causing suicide as some form of intentional homicide must be statutorily defined. (2) Intent is a high barâ€”beyond just wanting a result to occur.

  **Facts**:	 D and V were drinking heavily. D was mad at V for having sex with his wife. He encouraged V to kill himself. He gave V a gun with 5 shells and left him. V shot himself. 

**Holding/Rationale:** (1) No homicide took place because the defendant did not kill another person. (2) Defendant had no present intention to kill. D hoped that V would kill himself but hope alone is not the degree of intention requisite to a charge of murder.

* Dâ€™s conduct is morally reprehensible, but it is not criminal.  
* Suicide, by definition, is not homicide (killing a person by an act of another). The simple overt act of providing the means for killing doesnâ€™t amount to a homicide.  
*  â€œHopeâ€ is insufficient for a charge of murder.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Transferred Intent**  
  * The doctrine of transferred intent takes defendantâ€™s intent to kill A and transfers it to the action that killed B. **MPC 2.03(2)(a)** provides that where the crime requires that a D intentionally cause a particular result (e.g. killing someone), that element of the crime is satisfied if the D accidentally inflicts that injury on one person while intentionally trying to injure another.  
* **State v. Contua-Ramirez (Arizona Appellate 1986\) p. 585**  
  * D, attempting to strike his wife, accidentally hit their baby whom she was holding in her arms.   
  * **Holding:** Convicted of intentionally injuring the child, which was a felony when hitting the wife would have been a misdemeanor. 

## People v. Kevorkian (MI., 1994\) â€“ assisted suicide {#people-v.-kevorkian-(mi.,-1994)-â€“-assisted-suicide}

## â€œD must be involved in the final overt act to be convicted of murder; involvement in events prior not enoughâ€ {#â€œd-must-be-involved-in-the-final-overt-act-to-be-convicted-of-murder;-involvement-in-events-prior-not-enoughâ€}

**Rule**:	A conviction for murder is proper if a defendant participates in the final overt act that causes death of another but not if the defendant is merely involved in the events leading up to the commission of the final overt act.

**Facts**:	A doctor allegedly helped two people commit suicide, and he was charged with two counts of murder. Assisted suicide 

**Holding/Rationale:** Although D was a but-for and proximate cause of Vs death, reluctance to hold D liable for murder when someone elseâ€™s *independent* decision ultimately led to death. The matter was remanded to the circuit court for considerations of Kevorkianâ€™s motion to quash the indictment.

* Their death was foreseeable, but his actions werenâ€™t the actual cause â€“ there was an intervening cause that absolves him of responsibility  
* Pushing the needle (pulling the trigger) \= murder. Furnishing the poison or the gun (aka providing the means) \= assisted suicide 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Here, suicide machine was set up by D, but machines allowed women to be last person to make the choice of whether or not they wanted to die. **Voluntary act\!**  
* Distinction b/w active participation in a suicide & involvement in events leading up to suicide

| Capital punishment  |
| :---: |

**MPC Â§ 210.6 â€“ Sentence of Death for Murder; Further Proceedings to Determine Sentence**

* (1) Circumstances that exclude imposition of the death penalty  
* (2) Procedural requirements for a death sentence, namely a separate proceeding  
* (3) Aggravating circumstances  
  * Committed by convict under sentence of imprisonment  
  * Previously convicted of another murder  
  * Committed another murder at the same time  
  * Knowingly created a great risk of death to many persons  
  * Committed while engaging in a violent felony  
  * Committed for the purpose of avoiding or preventing a lawful arrest or effecting an escape from lawful custody  
  * Committed for pecuniary gain  
  * Especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, manifesting exceptional depravity   
* (4) Mitigating circumstances  
  * No significant history of prior criminal activity  
  * Committed while defendant was influence of extreme mental or emotional disturbance  
  * Victim was participant in defendantâ€™s homicidal conduct or consented to homicidal act  
  * Committed under circumstances which the defendant believed to provide a moral justification or extenuation for his conduct  
  * Accomplice in a murder committed by another person and his participation was relatively minor  
  * Acted under duress or under the domination of another person  
  * At time of murder, capacity of defendant to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct or to conform his conduct to requirements of law was impaired by mental disease or intoxication  
  * Youth of the defendant at time of murder

# Death Penalty Jurisprudence {#death-penalty-jurisprudence}

**The Current Context**

* All the rules and framework that surround the death penalty do not serve to prevent the innocent from being executed, but instead create a framework that make it possible for the public to think that the death penalty is protected by sufficient safeguards.  
* Tendency to focus on the doctrinal nuances, but also important to step back and analyze how these contribute to the overall political economy currently in society  
* **Periods of Jury Discretion**  
  * Antebellum period: automatic death penalty  
  * 1860 â€“ 1960s: absolute jury discretion  
  * Post-*Furman*/*Gregg*: guided discretion  
* **Harcourtâ€™s** opposition to death penalty primarily based on application and procedures  
* Even though a state may have abolished the death penalty (25 states in total have), a federal prosecutor can still prosecute someone under federal law in all 50 states and territories.   
* **Cost of punishment:** Capital punishment is substantially more expensive than non-capital prosecution  
* Statistics  
  * 16 states (and DC) do not authorize the death penalty under any circumstance  
  * Many others produce very little death sentences  
  * 5 death penalty states have had no executions in the modern era  
  * **FLORIDA, MISSOURI, OKLAHOMA, TEXAS AND VIRGINIA accounted for roughly â…” of all executions between 1977-2009**  
    * **Texas is responsible for more than â…“ of execution**  
  * Some states issue a lot of death sentences, but carry out very few executions (eg: CA has roughly 670 people on death row, but has only executed about 13 since 1976\)  
  * More states are beginning to either abolish it or put a moratorium on it while they audit their criminal justice procedures 

**Constitutional Limitations** 

* Litigation challenging the death penalty focused on two issues:  
  * Â (a) Cruel and Unusual Punishment (8th Amendment)  
  * Â (b) Procedural Due Process (14th Amendment)

**Cruel and Unusual Punishment**

* *Furman v. Georgia:* SCOTUS held that capital punishment, as then administered, violated 8th Ams prohibition of â€œcruel and unusual punishments,â€ but provided no majority opinion/guidelines as to how to execute capital punishment constitutionally. **Only thing that was certain was that absolute jury discretion had to go.**  
* Many states passed legislation to address the concerns raised in ***Furman v. Georgia*** (US SC 1972\) about the arbitrary or discriminatory application of the death penalty. Two approaches to create guided discretion:  
  * (1) enacting legislation to make capital punishment mandatory in certain cases; or   
  * (2) establishing guidelines to determine who would be subjected to capital punishment.

* Types of death penalty statutes:  
  * Â **(a) Pyramid statutes (GA, pg. 540\)**  
    * Murder (base), aggravated murder (death eligible), aggravating circumstance, death (top)  
    * Jury unsure of why it is determining the death penalty for one person and not another  
    * Provides guidance and add consistency, but may be individualized  
    * Discretion is all the way at the top of the pyramid (death) and in the hands of the jury  
    * Upheld as constitutional by SC in *Gregg*  
  * Â **(b) Weighing statutes (AL)**  
    * (1) Aggravating circumstances, (2) Mitigating circumstances, (3) Jury must balance aggravating and mitigating circumstances, and (4) No mere number counting  
    * â€œHeinous atrocious, or cruel compared to other capital offensesâ€¦â€; this aggravating circumstance was found to be too vague and has been refined  
    * Provides guidance and is individualized, but may be inconsistent  
    * Does allow for judge â€œjudicial overrideâ€ (overrides juries decision)  
  * **(c) Special Question statutes (TX)â€¦*See Jurek***  
    * Â (1) Did the D act deliberately to kill?  
    * (2) Is it probable the defendant would commit violent crimes in the future  
    * Â (3) If provoked, was it reasonable?Â   
    * Special question statutes provide guidance, may be inconsistent and lack individuality; US SC later struck down as unconstitutional  
    * A fourth question was subsequently added to alleviate constitutional concerns  
      * (4) Should the defendant live or die when mitigating circumstances are considered? *See Penry*  
        * Weighing involved here  
  * Â **(d) Mandatory DP statutes (NC)**  
    * Does not allow for individualized sentencing; struck down as unconstitutional by the US SC  
      * Eg: â€œshall be punished with deathâ€

* **In the post-*Gregg* era, the court creates 2 requirements: (1) consistency in sentencing and (2) individualized sentencing.**  
  * These are very much at odds w/ each other  
  * Scalia and Thomas severed off individualized sentencing in death penalty context (said was made up in Lockett v. Ohio)  
  * Blackmun said he couldnâ€™t do death penalty anymore b/c of this consistency vs. individualized sentencing thing.

**Procedural Due Process**

* *McGautha v. California (1971)*: SCOTUS rejected idea that capital punishment required explicit criteria to be in accord with due process. They said that discretion of the jury to decide by themselves is not unconstitutional.  
  * Death penalty unsuccessfully challenged under the Due Process Clause of the 14th Amendment in *McGautha v. California* which held, â€œcommitting to the untrammeled discretion of the jury the power to pronounce life or death is \[not\] offensive to anything in the Constitution.â€  
    * Argued that jury discretion is necessary and inevitable, impossible to have standards for death

**DP Procedure Today**

* Bifurcated proceedings: guilt phase versus penalty phase (same jury)  
* Judge or jury sentencing (or both)  
* Aggravators / mitigators or special questions that the jury will have to decideÂ   
* Individualized sentencing determination  
* Appellate review  
* Stages of collateral review (habeas corpus in federal and post-conviction appeal in state)  
* Recent decisions have been focused on carving out ability to apply death penalty:  
  * (1) Cannot apply it to non-murder cases  
  * (2) Cannot apply to individuals with mental retardation (*Atkins*)  
  * (3) Cannot apply it to juveniles

## Gregg v. Georgia (GA., 1976\) â€“ death penalty is constitutional. No violation of 8th and 14th Ams.  {#gregg-v.-georgia-(ga.,-1976)-â€“-death-penalty-is-constitutional.-no-violation-of-8th-and-14th-ams.}

## â€œto be constitutional, death penalty sentencing cannot be arbitrary or disproportionateâ€ {#â€œto-be-constitutional,-death-penalty-sentencing-cannot-be-arbitrary-or-disproportionateâ€}

**Rule**:	The death penalty is not a per se violation of the Eighth and Fourteenth Amendments but should be imposed under sentencing procedures to avoid capricious or indiscriminate use.

**Facts**:	 Gregg convicted of death-eligible homicides. The jury imposed the death penalty pursuant to statutory guidelines.

**Holding/Rationale:**  The death penalty is not per se violation of the 8th (cruel and unusual punishment) and the 14th (equal protection/due process) Amendment. Georgiaâ€™s death penalty statute does not violate the Constitution

* Under *Furman*, the imposition of the death penalty cannot be arbitrary or capricious.  
* Here, we have a carefully drafted statute that ensures the sentencing authority has enough information and guidance.  
* Georgiaâ€™s statute (described on pp. 570â€“572) is adequately particularized to the defendantâ€™s circumstances  
  * GA statute NOT arbitrary b/c it requires a jury to find a statutory aggravating circumstance before recommending death penalty.

**Dissent (Marshall):** Capital punishment has no utilitarian or retributivist justifications. It is not necessary as a deterrent to our society when we have life imprisonment and the morality in taking a murdererâ€™s life is insufficient, as plurality says, because public values are not dispositive of the 8th Am and capital punishment violates wrongdoerâ€™s dignity.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* SC upheld capital punishment given that society had seemed to endorse the death penalty and that it served the goals of deterrence and retribution.  
* However, the court found it must not be â€œexcessive.â€ Inquiry into **â€œexcessivenessâ€** has two aspects:  
  * (1) Punishment must not involve unnecessary or wanton infliction of pain  
  * (2) Must not be grossly out of proportion to severity of the crime

# Modern jurisprudence {#modern-jurisprudence}

## Atkins v. Virginia (VA., 2002\) â€“ Theoretical justifications for not imposing death penalty on mentally disabled {#atkins-v.-virginia-(va.,-2002)-â€“-theoretical-justifications-for-not-imposing-death-penalty-on-mentally-disabled}

## â€œcapital punishment of intellectually disabled D violates 8th Amendmentâ€ {#â€œcapital-punishment-of-intellectually-disabled-d-violates-8th-amendmentâ€}

**Rule**:	Capital punishment of a mentally retarded individual constitutes cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the Eighth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution.

**Facts**:	Atkins was convicted of a death-eligible murder. An expert witness testified that he was â€œmildly mentally retarded.â€ Evidence showed he had an IQ of only 59\. Atkins was sentenced to death.

**Holding/Rationale:** Death penalty for mentally retarded defendant is cruel and unusual punishment under the Eighth Amendment

* **Retribution:** It is consensus that mentally retarded/intellectually handicapped people are less culpable for their conduct than those with â€œnormalâ€ intellectual capacities. Administering like punishment for unequal culpability offends retributive notions.  
* **Deterrence:** They are also less deterred because of their lesser ability to process information, control impulses, etc., which offends notions of deterrence.  
* **Procedural Risks:** (1) less capable of providing meaningful assistance to counsel to defend their cases. (2) improper confessions (3) jury prejudice   
* SCOTUS, observing that even in those states that allow the execution of a mentally retarded criminal the practice is uncommon, noted that the practice of such executions â€œhas become truly unusualâ€ and that it was fair to say that a â€œnational consensusâ€ had developed against it.

**Dissent (Scalia):** Says courtâ€™s evidence for national consensus is poor (not supported by text or history or 8th Am. or current social attitudes sufficiently) and a blanket for courtâ€™s abuse of power. Believes retribution depends not only on the moral culpability of the defendant, but also the depravity of the crime. Deterrence is justified as long as many, not all, can be deterred.

# Race and the Death Penalty {#race-and-the-death-penalty}

## McCleskey v. KeMp (SCOTUS, 1987\) â€“ showing of general discriminatory impact is insufficient {#mccleskey-v.-kemp-(scotus,-1987)-â€“-showing-of-general-discriminatory-impact-is-insufficient}

## â€œevidence must prove existence of discriminatory purpose and racially disproportionate effect using evidence specific to caseâ€ {#â€œevidence-must-prove-existence-of-discriminatory-purpose-and-racially-disproportionate-effect-using-evidence-specific-to-caseâ€}

**Rule**:	A criminal defendant alleging 14th Am EP violation must prove the existence of a discriminatory purpose and a racially disproportionate and discriminatory effect using evidence specific to their case. A showing of general discriminatory impact is insufficient. 

**Facts**:	McCleskey challenged GAs capital sentencing process, alleging they were administered in a racially discriminatory manner, thus violating 8th and 14th Ams. Submitted Baldus study, which showed that black defendants who killed white victims have greatest likelihood of receiving the death penalty even when isolating 230 variables that couldâ€™ve explained on nonracial grounds. 

**Holding/Rationale:** No EP violation b/c McCleskey did not prove discriminatory intent. Baldus study only shows disparate impact and is not specific to his case. The study failed to demonstrate a constitutionally significant risk of racial bias in GAâ€™s capital-sentencing process.

1. 14th Am EP Clause  
   1. disparate impact vs. discriminatory intent: court says must prove discriminatory intent in addition to disparate impact. The statute must be motivated â€œbecause ofâ€ not â€œin spite ofâ€ racial animus.  
      1. Problem \= impossibly high burden to satisfy intent b/c responsibility is so diffuse it is hard to identify locus of intent  
   2. Facial vs. as-applied challenge: McCleskey brought writ of habeas corpus claim (as- applied) so he needs evidence specific to his case  
2. 8th Am Cruel and Unusual Punishment  
   1. At what point is risk constitutionally unacceptable?  
      1. Baldus study does not constitute constitutionally significant risk of racial bias.  
      2. Court says discretion is necessary to criminal justice system  
      3. Focus on practical effect (legal realism) of deciding McCleskey other way; court is worried about floodgate of cases and no limiting principle if they rule in favor of McCleskey  
   2. **Harcourt:** paradox is that capital punishment is not arbitrary b/c there is a pattern based on race, but is also arbitrary b/c race shouldnâ€™t be a factor

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Reasoning Summarized**: (1) There is no evidence of discriminatory intent by the jury or the legislature. (2) Donâ€™t want to see too many of these challenges in federal court. (3) Problem better addressed to the legislature.  
* **Takeaway**: Prime example of the inadequacies of justice under **law**.  
  * If you take Powellâ€™s argument at face value, the (purely) constitutional argument, as framed, makes sense. You need evidence of discriminatory/invidious intent of the jury or the legislature.  
  * No evidence submitted indicating jury bias.  
  * The statute itself does not extend special protection to one class of people to the targeted detriment to another. No legislative history supporting such a contention.  
  * **POLICY:** But, is this really justice?   
    * Are you convinced that the political/legislative process in GA in 1987 would adequately correct this problem in the stateâ€™s capital-sentencing process?

| Justifications and Excuses |
| :---: |

* Two types of defenses:  
  * Prosecution fails to establish all elements beyond a reasonable doubt  
  * Some consideration that negates culpability   
    * Justifications (ex: self-defense)   
    * Excuses (ex: insanity) 

**Justifications and Excuse** 

* MPC has eliminated the distinction between justification and excuse   
* But people still think about and treat them differently: they have different implications for wrongdoing/blameworthiness 

**Justification**

* Means itâ€™s the right way to act, morally required.   
* **Legality** principle: need to spell it out in statutory language so people know how to act in a justifiable manner   
* **Burden of proof** is on prosecution, because it is an element  
* E.g. Self-Defense. Choice of Evils.  
* **Third party liability**: typically can bleed onto a third party.  
* **POLICY \- Theoretical rationales**  
  * â€œPublic benefitâ€ â€“ the offense is justifiable because society benefits from the actorâ€™s conduct  
  * â€œMoral forfeitureâ€ â€“ wrongdoing that violates the rights of another *forfeits* their relevant rights, so that they have no rights against self-defense by the intended victim   
  * â€œMoral rightsâ€ â€“ Flip-side of forfeiture. Conduct is justified when it is done in protection of a natural moral right in the defendant (e.g. life, autonomy, etc.)  
  * â€œSuperior Interestâ€ â€“ utilitarian. The law privileges whichever actor can claim the â€œSuperior interestâ€ so that the protection of important moral interests is maximized

**Excuse**

* Something excusable, like a human mistake or error  
* Not morally required, may be a moral failure  
* **Legality**: Doesnâ€™t need to be spelled out in statutory language because we donâ€™t want to encourage it and since people had no alternative  
* **Burden of proof** is on the defense   
* E.g. Necessity. Insanity.  
* **Third party** cannot benefit from someone elseâ€™s insanity defense.  
* **POLICY \- Theoretical rationales**:  
  * Deterrence â€“ excused if the behavior canâ€™t be deterred.  
  * Causation â€“ excuse if the actor didnâ€™t have full control over causing or not causing a certain result  
  * Blameworthiness â€“ excuse where the actor is not blameworthy or â€œbad.â€  
  * Free choice â€“ responsibility presupposes an autonomous decision. No blame for acts people donâ€™t freely choose.

The distinction has ripple effects in the specific doctrines. MPC discarded the strict distinction between the two.

# Self Defense â€“ Reasonableness \+ Necessity {#self-defense-â€“-reasonableness-+-necessity}

**Reasonableness \+ Necessity**

* When the use of defensive force against an aggressor is necessary to protect the actor from serious bodily harm, that action is justified â€“ encourage people to protect themselves, right thing to do.  
* If actorâ€™s reasonable belief turns out to be mistaken, one cannot say that use of force was justified; more precise to say that action is not justified but merely excused â€“ mistake was reasonable.  
* Typically requires a 1\) â€œreasonable beliefâ€ that the use of defensive force is 2\) necessary, and 3\) proportionate

\*What constitutes reasonable use of force in self-defense?

* consider **whether Ds belief** in need to use lethal force was **reasonable** with regards to its **necessity, imminence of harm,** and **proportionality.**  
* Focus on **time framing** to determine when reasonableness ends (when is lethal force no longer necessary) (think Eric Garner case)  
* Is reasonableness, a **subjective (MPC) or objective (common law)** inquiry?

**Standards of Judgment**  
Prevailing American rule is that a self-defense claim can succeed only when the defendantâ€™s fear and use of defensive force were both reasonable

* \*How does **subjective vs. objective standard** affect the time framing and evidence considered?  
  * MPC partially individualizes the objective standard   
    * Â§2.02: â€œThat a reasonable person would observe in the actorâ€™s situationâ€  
  * Majority of jurisdictions adopt some hybrid standard like MPC  
* When someone kills in the honest but unreasonable belief in the need to kill  
  * Generally prevailing view  
    * Person guilty of murder  
  * Some states follow doctrine of â€œimperfect self-defenseâ€  
    * Classifies crime as voluntary manslaughter on theory that malice is lacking, closer to heat of passion  
  * Even less common approach: classify killing as involuntary manslaughter  
  * MPC approach  
    * Guilty of negligent homicide  
    * Hasnâ€™t been influential in statutory reform

**Issues that arise in self-defense**

1. Reasonableness: subjective or objective standard?  
2. Imminence/immediacy of the harm (temporality of necessity; BWS)  
3. Necessity to protect from harm (*Peterson* â€“ gun v. wrench)  
4. Deadly force  
5. Provocation (whether person initiated actions requiring self-defense)  
6. Duty to retreat â€“ right to stand your ground (*Zimmerman* case)  
7. Mistake regarding deadly force  
8. Time framing: Courts generally take a narrow time frame   
* **Harcourt**: this is often improperly neglected. Self-defense is a defense of necessity, but necessity is time-bound, contextual, and very specific. The action may be necessary at one time (or second) and not another.   
* **POLICY:** See Kelman, â€œInterpretive Construction in the Substantive Criminal Lawâ€  
9. Level of harm that determines the level of self-defense you can apply  
* Deadly  
* Serious  
* Unlawful 

## 

## MPC Provisions {#mpc-provisions}

**MPC Â§ 3.04**

* (1) Subject to (2), (3), and MPC Â§ 3.09, the use of force is justifiable when the actor believes that such force is immediately necessary to protect himself against use of unlawful force.  
* (2) Limitations of justified use of force  
  * (a) Not justified  
    * (i) Resisting arrest which the actors knows is being made by a peace officer  
    * (ii) Resisting force by person claiming right of her property (with exceptions)  
  * (b) Deadly force not justified unless actor *believes* force is necessary to protect himself against death, serious bodily harm, kidnapping, or rape. Also not justifiable if:  
    * Actor provoked, with purpose of causing death or serious bodily harm, the use of force  
    * Actor knows he can avoid necessity of deadly force by retreating, surrendering property, or complying  
  * (c) Standard of review is force necessary under the circumstances as the actor believes them to be when the force is used  
    * No reasonableness requirement, but limited by MPC 3.09

**MPC Â§ 3.09 | VERY IMPORTANT**

* (1) Self-defense justification not available when the actorâ€™s belief as to the unlawfulness of the force being used against him (or unlawfulness of the arrest) is erroneous AND his error is due to ignorance or mistake as to the provisions of the MPC, any other provision of the criminal law, OR the law governing search and arrest.  
  * Aka If youâ€™re **reckless/negligent** in forming belief that force is necessary, donâ€™t charge you with murder, but charge you with reckless/negligent homicide

**MPC Â§ 3.11Â **

* (1) â€œunlawful forceâ€ is force, including confinement, which is employed without the personâ€™s consent and would constitute an offense or a tort.  
* (2) â€œdeadly forceâ€ is force which the actor uses with the purpose of causing or which he knows to create a substantial risk of causing death or seriously bodily harm.  
  * Purposely firing a gun in the direction of another person or at a vehicle in which another person is believed to be in constitutes deadly force.  
  * A threat of deadly force is not deadly force so long as the actorâ€™s purpose is limited to creating an apprehension of deadly force.  
    * You need some affirmative act that creates the substantial risk of death or serious bodily harm.

## U.S. v. Peterson (DC., 1973\) â€“ reasonable belief: narrow time framing {#u.s.-v.-peterson-(dc.,-1973)-â€“-reasonable-belief:-narrow-time-framing}

## â€œdeadly force in self-defense justified when the actorâ€™s belief is reasonableâ€ {#â€œdeadly-force-in-self-defense-justified-when-the-actorâ€™s-belief-is-reasonableâ€}

**Rule**:	The use of lethal force in self- defense is only justified when an actor **reasonably believes it is necessary** to use deadly force.

**Holding/Rationale:** Necessity is *contextual,* changes based on circumstances and time (temporality of necessity).

* Here, D cannot claim self-defense b/c he brought out gun first, which prompted V to take out wrench and advance. D is the initial aggressor\!  
* **Conditions for using deadly use of force (in self-defense):**  
  * (1) There must have been a threat, actual or apparent, of the use of deadly force against the actor;  
  * (2) Threat must have been unlawful and immediate;  
  * (3) Actor must have *believed* he was in imminent peril of death or serious bodily harm, and that his response was necessary to save himself (subjective); AND  
  * (4) These beliefs must have been *honestly entertained* and *objectively reasonable* in light of the circumstances (objective)

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* The first 2 factors are issues of pure fact.  
* The third factor is a purely subjective, which, for our purposes, is a near-impossible standard for the prosecution to overcome.  
* The fourth factor is purely objective, which is very difficult for the defense to prove.  
* **Note:** Because the *Peterson* inquiry uses two distinct prongs rather than collapsed into one factor, the meat of the analysis will be over whether the defendantâ€™s belief was reasonable.

## People v. Goetz (NY., 1986\) â€“ Reasonable belief: broad time framing (subway murder of black boys) {#people-v.-goetz-(ny.,-1986)-â€“-reasonable-belief:-broad-time-framing-(subway-murder-of-black-boys)}

## â€œblended reasonableness standardâ€”objectively reasonable person in dâ€™s circumstances (subjective)â€ {#â€œblended-reasonableness-standardâ€”objectively-reasonable-person-in-dâ€™s-circumstances-(subjective)â€}

**Rule**:	Reasonablyâ€ must be interpreted objectively and canâ€™t be solely based on defendantâ€™s own subjective interpretation. The objectivity of defendantâ€™s belief may be informed by circumstances of the case, e.g., past defendantâ€™s past experiences. 

* **TIP:** *Cf.* Kelman (time framing)  
    
  **Facts**:	Four youths were in a subway car with defendant, who held a concealed gun. He began carrying this gun after a mugging a few years prior. One of the youths walked up to defendant and demanded $5 from him. No evidence that the kids were armed, though some had screwdrivers in their coats. Defendant shot each of the kids, shooting twice at one whom he missed initially. He admitted to the shootings and told police he wouldâ€™ve shot more had he had more bullets. The alleged error was the prosecutorâ€™s instructions to the grand jury: Was the jury to determine based on what D himself believed or what a reasonable person believes? 

**Statute:** NYPL Â§ 35.15(1) A person may use physical force when and to the extent he *reasonably believes* such to be necessary to defend himself or a third person from what he *reasonably believes* to be the use or imminent use of unlawful physical force by such other person.

* **NYPL Â§ 35.15(2)** A person may not use deadly physical force unless he (a) *reasonably believes* that such other person is using or about to use deadly physical force, or (b) *reasonably* believes that such person is committing or attempting to commit a kidnapping, forcible rape, or robbery.

**Holding/Rationale:** Prosecutorâ€™s instruction to determine whether Dâ€™s conduct was that of a reasonable man in Dâ€™s situation was correct. NYPL requires reasonableness.

* NY adopted much of the MPC but crucially added â€œreasonablyâ€ before â€œbelievesâ€Â which demonstrates that they intended to vary from MPC.  
* Interpreting the statute to say â€œreasonable to himâ€ would render â€œreasonablyâ€ meaningless and would allow subjective views to completely exonerate him.  
* Lower courtsâ€™ interpretation would allow citizens to set their own standards for permissible use of force.  
* Reasonableness inquiry happens with regard to the actual circumstances of the particular incident, including past experiences of the defendant.  
* **Blended reasonableness standardâ€”objectively reasonable person in Dâ€™s circumstances (subjective)**

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Determination of reasonableness must be based on Dâ€™s circumstances:**  
  * Physical movements of potential assailant  
  * Any relevant knowledge D had about that person  
  * Physical attributes of all persons involved, including D   
  * Any prior experiences D had which could provide a reasonable basis for belief that another personâ€™s intentions were to injure or rob him or that the use of deadly force was necessary under the circumstances   
      
* **Harcourt â€“ TIME FRAMING:** NYPL 35.15(2)(b) robbery? (i) â€œreasonably believesâ€; (ii) from a time-framing perspective: even if someone could have reasonably believed that he would be the victim of robbery, after he shot the first round, the threat became nonexistent and D no longer entitled to self defense justification.  
  * **Timing and Necessity:** Broad and narrow time-framing from Kelman is also relevant here.   
    * Narrow: If they had looked at the moment Goetz fired his last shots (after the males were no longer trying to rob him), then heâ€™s more likely to be found guilty b/c it was not necessary.   
    * Broad: But, if you look at the longer time period (where they were trying to rob him), then heâ€™s less likely to be guilty and necessity is more a defense. Shows how the time period you look at matters for necessity.  
* **Harcourt:** One of the main issues is where to place the self-defense standard on the spectrum from objective (reasonable belief in the need to defend oneself) to subjective (subjective belief in the need to defend oneself); and how that relates to racial justice. 

**POLICY \- Race in *Goetz*:** Problem w/drawing inferences from stats showing greater crimes committed by black people b/câ€¦

* **Foucault, *Birth of the Prison.*** Punishing people for who they are, rather than what they did. In this case, we are punishing the victims for being black by not convicting Goetz and offering that moral retribution.

# Battered Women/Spouse SYndrome (BWS) â€“ reasonableness \+ Imminence {#battered-women/spouse-syndrome-(bws)-â€“-reasonableness-+-imminence}

**Reasonableness \+ Imminence**  
**Battered Women Syndrome:** women (spouse) who suffer from learned helpless that results from repeated physical and psychological abuse over an extended period of time by the dominant figure in their lives 

**Two Main Issues**

1. Imminence requirement â€“ wife has alternatives to run away and seek help, doesnâ€™t have to use violence  
2. Termination of battering episode to make wife aggressor who cannot avail herself of self defense

**Time Framing** is essential here\! Instant killing might look voluntary, but if we broaden the time frame to include history of abuse, the decision to kill appears less voluntary and more justified. 

**Note:** similar trauma to fearful delusions, which are not allowed to assert self-defense.

**Perfect v. Imperfect Self Defense (*Norman*)**

* **Perfect self-defense** â€“ satisfies standard perfectly and youâ€™re off the hook  
  * **Imperfect self-defense** â€“ reduces your culpability but doesnâ€™t erase it completely 

| Perfect  | Imperfect |
| :---- | :---- |
| D can be acquitted by reason of perfect self-defense when the evidence, viewed in light most favorable to D, tends to show that at the time of the killing it appeared to D and she believed it to be necessary to kill the decedent in order to save herself from imminent death or great bodily harm. Belief must be reasonable.  Perfect self-defense is always completely justified in law and constitutes no legal wrong. | Culpability is reduced, but D is not justified in the killing so as to be entitled to acquittal and is guilty of at least voluntary manslaughter. |

## 

## State v. Norman (NC., 1989\) â€“ imminence requirement issues (w shoots h while asleep) {#state-v.-norman-(nc.,-1989)-â€“-imminence-requirement-issues-(w-shoots-h-while-asleep)}

## â€œfuture harm doesnâ€™t satisfy imminence require for a self-defense claimâ€ {#â€œfuture-harm-doesnâ€™t-satisfy-imminence-require-for-a-self-defense-claimâ€}

**Rule**:	Inevitable (future) harm does not satisfy *imminence* requirement for self- defense claim

**Facts**:	Norman was badly abused during 25-year marriage. She shot her husband while he was asleep and was charged with first-degree murder. At trial, she presented evidence of suffering from battered wife syndrome as proof of a perfect self-defense. Jury convicted of voluntary manslaughter.

**Holding/Rationale:**  Self-defense is available only for imminent harm, not future harm. **Note:** Seems like a pretty narrow time frame. **Compare w/ *Goetz* (broad); Kelman.**

* Necessity defense only where defendant killed victim due to reasonable belief that death/great bodily harm was imminent  
* Evidence here doesnâ€™t show Norman reasonable believed she was confronted by threat of imminent death/great bodily harm.  
* There was ample time to resort to other means to prevent further abuse.  
* No action underway by decedent from which jury couldâ€™ve found reasonable grounds to believe an imminent danger.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **POLICY CONSIDERATIONS**  
  * It looks like, under *Norman*, that you canâ€™t argue an ongoing, consistent threat of abuse. The threat must be something more concrete and material at the time of the use of force.   
    * Does this holding actually reflect the reality of spouses who experience ongoing physical abuse? For all they know, the abusive spouse can be temperate at one moment and violent in another. Isnâ€™t it too late if abused spouses to have to wait until the last moment to react?Â   
    * If you agree with this line of reasoning, should the law allow *deadly* force in this situation? Itâ€™s one thing to fight back, and itâ€™s another to kill.  
* **Why Defenses Are Rejected Here**   
  * **Perfect and imperfect self-defense unavailable to D b/c she didnâ€™t satisfy imminence requirement** (shot husband while sleeping, couldâ€™ve left and gotten help). D is seen as initial aggressor and killing is an opportune killing rather than in self-defense.  
* Justification v. Excuse: perfect self-defense would be a justification insofar as there is no criminal liability; imperfect self-defense would make it an excuse (be charged with a lesser offense).  
  * Theoretical Q: Whether we want to think of the criminal law of having a place in these horrible social relations? Can we use criminal law to change peopleâ€™s behavior?

# Duty to Retreat {#duty-to-retreat}

**Duty to Retreat:** A person has a duty to retreat rather than use lethal force in self-defense when ***he knows*** that he can retreat in **complete safety.**

- Subjective NOT objective standard (about what D believed, not reasonable person)

**N.B. Both NYPL and MPC have duty to retreat provisions for use of deadly force**

**Traditional View**

* English common law imposed strict duty to retreat.   
* A person could use deadly force in self-defense only after exhausting every chance to flee, when his back was against the wall.

**Late 1800s View**

* The law will not permit the taking of a human life to repel mere trespass, but a true man who is without fault is not obliged to fly from an assailant. *See* *Erwin v. State* (Ohio 1876).

**Modern View**

* Most states adopted â€œtrue manâ€ /no-retreat rule/stand your ground rule. **Majority Approach**  
* Many states have passed â€œstand your groundâ€ laws that permit the actor to meet force with force, including deadly force, even when retreat is possible   
* MPC adopts a version of this rule with some exceptions.

**Castle Exception**

* In all jurisdictions requiring retreat before using deadly force, an exception is made when the defendant is attacked in her own home by an intruder. *See People v. Tomlin* (N.Y. 1914).  
  * Justification:  
    * **Defense of habitation.** Home-dweller is permitted to kill to protect sanctity of his home.  
    * **Sanctuary.** Home is viewed as personâ€™s final sanctuary from external attack, so home-dweller has already â€˜retreated as far as possibleâ€™  
* Usually, if the attacker is a guest of the homeowner, the exception will still apply ğŸ¡ªdeadly force justified

* Less clear if it applies between co-occupants of a home

  * *Tomkins*: father being threatened by his son could kill the son rather than retreat

  * Concern: fear that a duty to retreat would adversely affect victims of domestic violence, b/c for battered women, separation or retreat can be the most dangerous time in the relationship

**MPC 3.04(2)(b)** The use of deadly force is not justifiable under this sectionâ€¦if:

* The actor, with the purpose of causing death or serious bodily harm, **provoked the use of force** against himself in the same encounter; or  
* The actor knows that he can avoid the necessity of using such force with complete safety by retreating or by surrendering possession of a thing to a person asserting a claim of right thereto or by complying with a demand that he abstain from any action which he has no duty to take, except that:  
  * The actor is not obliged to retreat from her dwelling, even if the aggressor is a co-dweller

**Stand Your Ground**

* Even if you could retreat, you can stay and use force  
* Erodes the necessity requirement for self-defense  
* If someone says that they were standing their own ground, law enforcement canâ€™t arrest unless you can show probable cause that the force used was unlawful  
* **Highly controversial**   
  * The burden shift makes it difficult to demonstrate that the individual standing their ground was doing so unlawfully  
* Rationale:

  * Sends message to law-abiding citizens that if they are attacked, the law is on their side  
  * Sends utilitarian message to criminals that they threaten innocent persons at their own risk

## Zimmerman Case (FL) â€“ implications of no duty to retreat in self defense cases {#zimmerman-case-(fl)-â€“-implications-of-no-duty-to-retreat-in-self-defense-cases}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€}

**Facts**:	Zimmerman charged w/2nd-degree murder (no premeditation). Florida has Stand Your Ground Law. Zimmerman saw Martin walking in his neighborhood on his way to his fatherâ€™s fiancÃ©â€™s house. Zimmerman called the police b/c he thought Martin, who was unarmed, was suspicious. Police told Zimmerman to stay in his car, but Zimmerman got out and pursued Martin. Zimmerman said to police, â€œthese assholes, they always get away.â€

**Stand Your Ground Statute:** â€œcanâ€™t arrest, detain, charge, or prosecute D claiming stand your ground UNLESS you have probable cause that force was unlawfulâ€

**Holding/Rationale:** Zimmerman found not guilty.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **No Duty to Retreat has significant impacts on self-defense**  
  * ğŸ¡ª makes self-defense less about necessity  
  * ğŸ¡ª makes self-defense broader  
  * ğŸ¡ª burden flips onto prosecutor to prove self-defense was NOT necessary beyond a reasonable doubt  
  * ğŸ¡ª pushes ball on who is in the right and who is in the wrong  
* BUT duty to retreat does exist in cases where you provoke the conflict   
* FL statute more similar to *Goetz* (reasonably believes)  
* **Harcourt:** not a typical SYG case, because Zimmerman didnâ€™t use the statute to protect him from prosecution  
  * Defense wanted to go to trial and defend on the basis of self defense, instead of using SYG during pre-trial hearing to stop the trial from happening.  
    * If using stand your ground, prosecution would have had to prove probable cause (usually a low bar)  
    * But because it goes to trial, the issue become whether there was a valid self defense   
* **Harcourt** believes that this case turned on provocation   
  * Two strongest points for prosecutor on provocation  
    * 1\) did Zimmerman actually exhaust means to get out of this?   
    * 2\) Is there no defense here because he was engaged in a forcible felony

# Law Enforcement Use of Lethal Force {#law-enforcement-use-of-lethal-force}

**Grand Jury**

* Grand juries are standing juries, drawn from regular jury pool, who hear evidence presented by prosecutors  
* **Purpose:** To determine if there is probable cause to indict (charge) a person of a crime  
* **Size:** ranges from 23 (federal) to 12 (some states)  
* **Procedure**  
  * Grand jury proceedings are closed to public, media and targets of investigation and their counsel.   
  * Usually, prosecutors preside over the process without a judge in the room. Prosecutors usually call 1-2 witnesses (maybe more), typically the reporting officer and V  
    * Prosecutor typically instructs jury on the law.   
* A grand jury never returns verdicts. It either true bills (sufficient evidence for indictment; i.e. the formal charge) or no bills (insufficient evidence for an indictment) an indictment.   
* **Standard:** probable cause (not a high bar): It requires only the â€œkind of fair probabilityâ€ on which reasonable and prudent \[people,\] not legal technicians, actâ€ (internal quotations omitted)  
* **Frequency of Indictments:** Generally, grand juries will issue indictments in most, if not all, cases.   
* **POLICY:** Grand juries are often an instrument of investigation. They play different functions  
* State level for ordinary felony and street crime  
  * Usually a pro forma, short hearing, usually with just the police officer and victim. Suspect isnâ€™t there unless theyâ€™re testifying (so usually not)  
* White collar crime   
  * Prosecutor will use GJ as a way to subpoena witnesses, documents, etc.Â Â   
* Police shootings  
  * Used as a way to mimic a trial (**Harcourt** \- this is troubling). Used in a way to make people believe that there was been a trial and to make people believe that thereâ€™s been a verdict  
    * Prosecutor tries to make people believe that the â€œtruthâ€ has been revealed  
      * **Harcourt** â†’ trials arenâ€™t about the truth. Theyâ€™re about whether there is enough evidence to meet a specific threshold.Â 

**Re Eric Garner**

* The grand jury was tasked with  
  * 1\) deciding whether there was sufficient evidence to believe that Officer Pantaleo violated any of the homicide provisions of the New York Penal Law and   
  * 2\) whether Officer Pantaleoâ€™s actions were justified as the legal use of force by a law enforcement officer in effecting an arrest  
* **Outcome:** Grand jury didnâ€™t indict Officer Pantaleo. Medical examiner ruled death a homicide.   
* Under NY Penal Law:  
  * **Relevant Statute: NY Penal Law 35.30.1(c):** Regardless of the particular offense which is the subject of the arrest or attempted escape, the use of deadly physical force is necessary to defend the police officer or peace officer or another person from what the officer reasonably believes to be the use or imminent use of deadly physical force.  
  * Garner violated a Cigarette and tobacco products tax law (See TAXÂ§ 1814\) by selling them individually and not paying taxes on them. At worst this is a misdemeanor.  
  * For purposes of use of non-lethal physical force, Pantaleo â€œmay use \[NON-LETHAL\] physical force when and to the extent he reasonably believes such to be necessary to effect the arrestâ€  
  * Chokehold seems to qualify for lethal force?  
    * Lethal force defined as anything that would cause serious physical injury or death  
    * But if not lethal, then donâ€™t get the deadly physical force provisions nor the limitation it imposes.   
    * **A defense arg:** maneuver was not deadly physical force therefore donâ€™t look at those provisions or arg there was an issue with causation   
* MPC (3.07 â€“ *Use of Force in Law Enforcement*) v. NY Penal Law   
  * **Exam Tip:** 3.07 deals with when its okay to use force as a cop. In 3.09, its more focused on whether the use is improper. Use 3.09 when you want to challenge use of force (reckless/negligent belief).   
  * Major difference between NY penal law â€“ reasonably belief standard, and MPC just says believes â€“ purely subjective  
    * MPC says you can use deadly force when there is a felony arrest (not misdemeanor). While MPC is purely subjective, we must consider 3.09 and the reasonableness standard (some objectivity)  
  * Args for whether actions were justified under MPC?   
    * Probably not: 3.07(b)(i): arrest is for a felony and (iv) the actor believes that:  
      * (1) the crime for which the arrest is made involved conduct including the use or threatened use of deadly force; or  
      * (2) there is a substantial risk that the person to be arrested will cause death or serious bodily harm if his apprehension is delayed.

# Necessity and Duress {#necessity-and-duress}

**General Notes**

1. **Necessity â€“ (mother nature) / Excuse**  
   1. Natural disasters â†’ we break law  
   2. Necessity excuses actions that would otherwise be impermissible  
   3. Medical necessity  
      1. Courts remain divided over whether to permit a necessity defense for medical uses of marijuana to alleviate the symptoms of severe illness  
         1. Even if states allow defense, still can be federally prosecuted  
      2. Many courts hostile to claims of economic necessity  
   4. EG: Mountain climbers take food/shelter in a blizzard  
2. **Choice of Lesser Evils â€“ (maximize welfare) / Justification**  
   1. Justification lens, utilitarian, consequentialist  
   2. Have to choice between 2 crimes  
   3. Someone (usually D) is threatened with serious harm and chooses (to extent that one chooses in a situation) to inflict harm in a way that would otherwise be deemed criminal.  
   4. E.g. Destroying property to stop a fire  
3. **Duress â€“ (human nature)**  
   1. Human based  
   2. E.g. someone is pointing gun at your head  
      

| Necessity | Choice of Evils | Duress |
| :---- | ----- | :---- |
| I had to do it (eg: political protest) | It was the better choice for society (eg: destroying prop to stop fire) | I was forced to do it (gun to head) |
| Excuse â€“ not a good act, but excused out of necessity (D caught in a position thatâ€™s not of their making) | Justified â€“ chose right thing to do out of 2 bad choices (crimes) | Excuse â€“ not a good act, but excused due to duress |
| Retributivist â€“ not blameworthy b/c necessary. Not social welfare maximizer.  | Utilitarian	â€“	maximize	social welfare. What is the right thing to do to maximize social utility? | Retributivist â€“ not blameworthy b/c forced. Not social welfare maximizer. |
| Subjective standard (stricter than choice of evils) | Objective standard (broader than necessity) | Subjective standard |
| External force can be natural |  | External force is human |
| Source of peril immaterial | \- | Source of peril must be â€œdo it or elseâ€ |

   \*Necessity, choice of evils, and duress are exceptions in criminal law. Should NOT be defense counselâ€™s first line of defense as they have more requirements and are looked upon less favorably.

**Necessity and Choice of Lesser Evils**

* Both are very similar; most jxs only have one of the two (in addition to a duress defense)   
* Classic example \= natural events. Some jxs (Wis.) limits to only this.  
* **Necessity Elements.** For necessity, there must be:  
  * A threat of  
    * Imminent injury to the person or property  
    * For which there are no (reasonable) alternatives except the commission of the crime;  
    * Dâ€™s acts must prevent an equal or more serious harm;  
    * D must not have created the conditions of his own dilemma.

## Regina v. Dudley & Stephens (QB., 1884\) â€“ Necessity and Duress Case Study â€“ \[obj./utiliarian\] (cannabalism on ship) {#regina-v.-dudley-&-stephens-(qb.,-1884)-â€“-necessity-and-duress-case-study-â€“-[obj./utiliarian]-(cannabalism-on-ship)}

## â€œnecessity is ONLY a defense in killings of self-defense; not a defense to preserving your own lifeâ€ {#â€œnecessity-is-only-a-defense-in-killings-of-self-defense;-not-a-defense-to-preserving-your-own-lifeâ€}

**Rule**:	The defense of necessity does not justify homicide unless the killing was committed in self-defense.

**Facts**:	Four men were cast adrift in a lifeboat in the Atlantic Ocean when the boat on which they had been sailing sank. After 19 days of very limited food, 2 Ds killed the cabin boy because he was the youngest, the only one without a family, and the weakest/sickest. The 3 survivors ate his corpse.

**Holding/Rationale:** Ds guilty of murder. There is no legal justification/necessity defense for the homicide, even if it was to preserve their own lives. Homicide may not be excused when the person killed is an innocent and unoffending victim.

* Where the victim has not assaulted or otherwise endangered the killer, the killer has not, by necessity, been placed in a position which permits him to kill the innocent victim.  
  * There is no â€œabsolute or unqualified necessity to preserve oneâ€™s life.â€ Doesnâ€™t matter if they would have died otherwise.   
* The defense of necessity does not justify homicide unless the killing was committed in self-defense.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Theories of Punishment**  
  * **Retribution**   
    * Theory of punishment is a retributive theory of punishment b/c there is a moral wrong-doing  
      * Dudley and Stephens violated a moral principle that is incorporated into English law  
  * **Utilitarian/ Welfare Maximizing Analysis**  
    * May also punish based on utilitarian/consequentialist perspective as a deterrence mechanism  
    * Contrast act (apply to this scenario only) vs. rule utilitarian (apply maximization across the board; all subsequent scenarios)  
    * Is cannibalism a useful device to keep people alive in the seas? Does it maximize welfare? Raises enormous questions of consequentialism. What is the most useful sacrifice of life? Not clear that cabin boy was most useful sacrifice of life. How do you measure life potential (age, health, skills necessary to survive)? **Harcourt:** This raises very uncomfortable decisions on value of human life.  
* In England at the time, there was no other necessity aside from self defense	

## Necessity {#necessity}

**Common Law Requirements**

* A threat of imminent injury to the person or property,  
  * Whatâ€™s imminent? *See, e.g.*, *Norman*.  
  * Traditionally natural causes like a blizzard  
* No (reasonable) alternatives except the commission of the crime,  
  * **Standard:** An objective standard independent of the defendantâ€™s beliefs  
* The defendantâ€™s otherwise criminal conduct would prevent an equal or more serious harm, AND  
  * Choice of evils: Use the same or less harm/evil/force or suffer the threat of injury  
* The defendant hasnâ€™t created the conditions of his own dilemma.  
  * For example, the defendant couldnâ€™t have provoked the incoming harm.

**MPC Â§ 3.02: Justification Generally: Choice of Evils**   
(1) Conduct which the actor believes to be necessary to avoid a harm or evil to himself or to another is justifiable, provided that:

* (a) the harm or evil sought to be avoided is greater than that sought to be prevented by the law defining the offense charged; **choice of evils provision**  
* (b) neither the Code nor other law defining the offense provides exceptions or defenses dealing with the specific situation involved; AND  
  * If thereâ€™s a defined self-defense provision that applies to your facts (e.g. use of deadly force or law enforcement defendant), you must use that provision.   
    * This makes 3.02 a catch-all provision for whatever else the MPC doesnâ€™t specifically cover.  
* (c) a legislative purpose to exclude the justification claimed does not plainly appear  
  * You can point to prong (b) to argue no contrary legislative purpose.

(2) When the actor was reckless or negligent in bringing about the situation requiring a choice of harms or evils, the necessity defense is unavailable for conduct involving the mens rea of recklessness or negligence.   
**THERE IS NO NECESSITY DEFENSE IF THE ACTOR WAS NEGLIGENT OR RECKLESS IN CREATING THE SITUATION.**

**NOTE:** **Important points for MPC 3.02**

* **Standard is both subjective and objective.** The belief of necessity is purely subjective, but the (sub-)prongs are purely objective.  
  * Does the person believe their illegal conduct is necessary to avoid harm/evil? (Subjective)  
  * Was the harm caused by breaking this law more or less than the harm avoided? (Objective)  
* Does not require imminence  
* Does not restrict the defense to situations involving a threat of death or serious bodily harm  
  * Ordinary harm is fine to the extent that the harm you inflict is equal to or less than avoided harm. **Cost benefit analysis.**   
* Defense is available in a homicide  
  * Unlike in *Dudley and Stephens*

The objective v. subjective weighing is where the utilitarianism v. retribution comes in.

1. **Objective Weighing \[utilitarian/ wholesale\]** ğŸ¡ª The comparison of the two harms is determined entirely by pre-determined societal/legal calculus. There is a right answer. It is not a question of whether the defendantâ€™s weighing makes sense. The question is whether the weighing was right. *(Regina v. Dudley and Stephens â€“ cannibalism on ship)*  
2. **Subjective Weighing \[retributive/ individualâ€™s moral blame\]** ğŸ¡ª The question is whether the defendantâ€™s weighing of the two harms was reasonable. Not necessarily correct according to a pre-fixed, external standard. So long as the defendantâ€™s weighing was reasonable, they are not blameworthy/they donâ€™t owe any sort of moral debt. *(People v. Unger â€“ prison escape)*

## People v. Unger (IL., 1977\) â€“ Necessity defense in prison escape interpreted as choice of evils  {#people-v.-unger-(il.,-1977)-â€“-necessity-defense-in-prison-escape-interpreted-as-choice-of-evils}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-1}

**Rule**:	Conduct which would otherwise be an offense is justifiable by reason of necessity if the defendant was without blame in developing the situation and reasonably believed such conduct was necessary to avoid a public or private injury greater than the injury which might result from his own conduct.

**Facts**:	Defendant was an inmate at a state prison (farm) & was subjected to threats of sexual violence and actual sexual violence. Defendant sought help but escaped after he received death threats. He claimed that he planned to return once he could find help.

**IL Statute on Necessity** ğŸ¡ª Conduct which would otherwise be an offense is justifiable by reason of **necessity** if 1\) the accused was without blame in occasioning or developing the situation and 2\) reasonably believed such conduct was **necessary** to avoid a public or private injury greater than the injury which might reasonably result from his own conduct.â€ 

**Holding/Rationale:** The facts are sufficient to entitled D **to raise an affirmative necessity defense for jury consideration.** *Lovercamp* factors for prison escape are instructive, but not dispositive. Incarcerated people are entitled to the same necessity defense as everyone else. 

* IL statute sets out a necessity defense, but the court interprets it as a choice of evils  
  * By casting it as a choice of evils, court rejects duress as appropriate b/c D was not â€œdeprived of his free will,â€ which the court thought was what distinguishes â€˜necessityâ€™ from â€˜duressâ€™; here, D had a choice, albeit â€œbetween 2 evilsâ€ (for necessity defense, need absolutely no free will)

* *Lovercamp* conditions for when a necessity defense in prison escape would hold:

  * 1\) Prisoner faced with **specific threat** of death/forcible sexual attack/substantial bodily injury in the **immediate future**

  * 2\) **No time** for a complaint to authorities or there exists history of futile complaints  
  * 3\) **No time** or opportunity to resort to courts  
  * 4\) No evidence of force or violence used towards prison personnel or other innocent persons in escape

  * 5\) Prisoner **immediately reports** to proper authorities when he is safe from immediate threat

    * strongest fact for state, BUT remember that Unger didnâ€™t change out of his jumpsuit, which suggests that he wasnâ€™t trying to hide from authorities. 

**DISSENT:** Recognizing this necessity defense for prison escape could encourage more future escapes, disrupt prison   
discipline, and result in injury to prison guards, police or private citizens. Necessity defense should exist, but only be recognized when all *Lovercamp* elements are met.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* IL statute says reasonably believes while the MPC doesnâ€™t have reasonable language ğŸ¡ª subjective belief but has reckless/negligence in second part

## U.S. v. Schoon (9th Cir., 1992\) â€“ Four factors for proving necessity {#u.s.-v.-schoon-(9th-cir.,-1992)-â€“-four-factors-for-proving-necessity}

## â€œno necessity defense for indirect civil disobedienceâ€ {#â€œno-necessity-defense-for-indirect-civil-disobedienceâ€}

**Rule**:	Unavailable in indirect civil disobedience cases, the necessity defense requires a defendant to show that he was (1) faced with a choice between two related evils, (2) acted to prevent imminent harm, (3) reasonably anticipated a direct causal relationship between his conduct and the harm to be avoided and (4) there was no legal alternative to violating the law.

**Facts**:	A group of individuals, who entered and vandalized an IRS office to protest U.S. involvement in El Salvador, claimed the necessity defense when criminally charged for these acts.

**Holding/Rationale:** The necessity defense is unavailable as a matter of law to one committing indirect civil disobedience for humanitarian purposes.

* **Four necessary elements to prove necessity:**  
  * (1) Faced with a choice of evils and choose the lesser evil  
  * (2) Acted to prevent imminent harm  
  * (3) Reasonably anticipated a direct causal relationship between their conduct and the harm to be avoided  
  * (4) No legal alternatives to violating the law  
* Necessity is inapplicable in cases involving indirect civil disobedience  
  * Protestors were not challenging the laws under which they were charged â€“ just calling attention; Congress had to act; other ways to encourage this  
  * Necessity is a utilitarian defense, maximizing social welfare by allowing a crime to be committed where the social benefits of the crime outweigh the social costs of failing to commit the crime 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Direct v. indirect civil disobedience  
  * Direct ğŸ¡ª violation of a law to protest its existence (eg: civil rights lunch counter protest)  
  * Indirect ğŸ¡ª violation of a law that is not the object of the protest   
    * In this case, Ds were not protesting the laws under which they were charged. They violated the laws to bring attention to US policy

## Duress {#duress}

**Common Law Requirements**

* (1) Well-founded fear generated by a human being,  
  * Fear must be reasonable  
  * **Contrast:** Note difference from necessity, which traditionally required a natural threat  
* (2) Threat of imminent death or serious bodily harm to the actor, AND  
  * Most states recognize a third-person provision to the threat, i.e. â€œto the actor or a third person.â€  
* (3) The defendant hasnâ€™t created or provoked the threat of death or serious bodily injury

**Note: Once the threat stops, the criminal conduct must stop.**

**MPC Â§ 2.09 \- Duress**  
(1) It is an affirmative defense that the actor was coerced to do so by the use of, or a threat to use, unlawful force against his person or third person, which a person of reasonable firmness in his situation wouldâ€™ve been unable to resist

* This is slightly less than a reasonableness standard because the statute adds â€œin his situation.â€ 

(2) This defense is unavailable if the actor recklessly placed himself in a situation in which it was probable that he would be subjected to duress. The defense is also unavailable if he was negligent in placing himself in such a situation, whenever negligence suffices to convict.

* **Note** that if you are reckless in placing yourself in that situation, you canâ€™t use the defense for *any* charge. Negligently placing yourself in that situation bars the defense for negligent crimes.

(3) It is not a defense that a woman acted on the command of her husband, unless she acted under coercion under (1).

* Obviously a gendered statute among many in the MPC, but would likely apply to any spouse, especially in this day and age.   
* Provision should be read (and accordingly applied) as prohibiting the simple fact of marriage as indicating coercion. You need more than â€œmy spouse told me to do it.â€

(4) When the conduct of the actor would otherwise be justifiable under MPC 3.02 (choice of evils), this section doesnâ€™t preclude a defense. 

| DURESS MPC vs. COMMON LAW |  |  |
| ----- | :---- | :---- |
|  | **MPC 2.09** | **COMMON LAW** |
| **Homicide** | Duress is complete defense in homicide cases | Denies duress defense in homicide cases *\*NJ takes intermediate position and mitigates	sentence	from	murder	to manslaughter (most common)* |
| **Nature	of threat** | Broader than common law. **Use or threats of force against his person** (ie. not extended to property, but doesnâ€™t require death/serious physical injury) | Only threat of **death or serious bodily harm** can give rise to duress defense |
| **Imminence** | NO imminence requirement, but will treat it as factor to consider for reasonableness inquiry | YES,	imminence	is	an	absolute prerequisite to duress defense |

| MPC 3.02 Necessity & Choice of Evils | MPC 2.09 Duress |
| :---- | :---- |
| Source of peril immaterial | Source of peril essential\! Duress defense only arises from â€œdo-it-or-elseâ€ command of another person. |
| Source of peril can be a natural event | Source of peril can NOT be a natural event |

## State v. Toscano (NJ., 1977\) â€“ ordinary fortitude standard for duress {#state-v.-toscano-(nj.,-1977)-â€“-ordinary-fortitude-standard-for-duress}

## â€œthreatened injury must induce fear that would cause a man of ordinary fortitude to yieldâ€ {#â€œthreatened-injury-must-induce-fear-that-would-cause-a-man-of-ordinary-fortitude-to-yieldâ€}

**Rule**:	To excuse a crime using a duress defense, the threatened injury must induce â€œsuch a fear as a man of ordinary fortitude and courage might justly yield to.â€

**Facts**:	Toscano was charged with insurance fraud but argued that he was under duress. He claimed that he was fearful of harm to him and his wife by the alleged architect of the conspiracy. After event, moved to new address, changed numbers, etc. 

**Holding/Rationale:** Toscano is entitled to a duress jury instruction. 

* New Jersey doesnâ€™t have a duress statute, so we use common law principles.  
  * The threat of harm must be â€œpresent, imminent and pendingâ€ and â€œof such a nature as to induce a well-grounded apprehension of death or serious bodily harm if the act is not done.â€  
  * To excuse a crime, the threatened injury must induce â€œsuch a fear as a man of ordinary fortitude and courage might justly yield to.â€  
  * Loss of job, denial of food rations, economic need, and prospect of financial ruin are inadequate.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Concern for the well-being of another, particularly a near relative, can support a defense of duress if the other requirements are satisfied. Defendantâ€™s testimony provided a factual basis for a finding that the ringleader threatened him and his wife with physical violence if he refused to assist in the fraudulent scheme.   
* Under MPC Â§2.09   
  * Key would to be argue about whether a person of reasonable firmness in Dâ€™s situation would have been unable to resist   
* **Timing:** If threat is more imminent, seems more like duress. The longer the threat, the more we shift into realm of necessity 

## U.S. v. Fleming (Military ApP. Ct., 1957\) â€“ Must have immediacy for duress (military case) {#u.s.-v.-fleming-(military-app.-ct.,-1957)-â€“-must-have-immediacy-for-duress-(military-case)}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-2}

**Rule**:	Danger of death or serious bodily harm must be immediate for a duress defense.

**Facts**:	 Defendant was court martialed for collaborating with Korean combatants when he was held captive. The alleged collaboration occurred after forced marches, physical abuse, and threats of being left for dead in a cave. The military judge instructed that duress was available only if he had a well-grounded apprehension of immediate and impending death or of immediate serious bodily harm. Fleming was convicted.

**Holding/Rationale:** Defendant is not entitled to the duress defense.

* Flemingâ€™s resistance had not brought him to the last ditch; the danger of death or serious bodily harm was not immediate.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Very high bar. If being a POW doesnâ€™t present a (constant) threat of immediate harm, what will?  
* **TIP:** This case is really easy to distinguish in your fact patterns.  
  * Military courts donâ€™t really follow the same procedural rules and substantive norms as civil(ian) courts, especially during wartime.   
  * Potentially a remnant of the Red Scare/McCarthyism.


  


# Insanity {#insanity}

Basis for insanity defense is the *opposite* of duressâ€”it is not that actor is no different than the rest of us b/c he was objectively reasonable; it is that the actor is **so different** than the rest of us that it warrants different treatment. 

**When to use:** To use the insanity defense in a jx that allows it, the actor must have been suffering from mental illness AT THE TIME OF THE CRIME. **MPC Â§4.01**

Rationale:

* **Utilitarian**. Person who is insane cannot be deterred; incapacitation not necessary as acquittal most likely results in civil commitment; rehabilitation is not furthered by prison b/c person has medical problem\!  
* **Retributive**. Just punishment is dependent on moral desert ğŸ¡ª moral desert is dependent on moral responsibility for oneâ€™s actions ğŸ¡ª moral responsibility for oneâ€™s actions is dependent on rationality and self-control Ã  insane people lack rationality and self-control\!

2 general problems with insanity defense:

* (1) Rarely asserted, but often raised in most heinous of cases ğŸ¡ª conflict b/w desire to punish & intuitive sense that punishment of such persons is inappropriate  
* (2) War b/w lawyers & psychiatrists: to what extent should criminal law be beholden and responsive to medical concepts and its relevant changes?

Who can raise the defense?

* Both prosecution and defense\!  
* This is potentially problematic b/c if D does not want to raise insanity defense (b/c can lead to longer confinement, more intrusive treatment, and stigma), but prosecutor enters evidence of such and court rules not guilty due to insanity, then sometimes canâ€™t appeal b/c there was no conviction\! *(See Hughes v. State, (Ark., 2011, pg. 964).*

Burden of proof/persuasion is on D

* After *Hinckley* (Regan assassination attempt), about Â¾ states place burden of proof on D  
* 12 states require prosecution to prove sanity beyond reasonable doubt  
* About 75% states place burden on defendants (usually by preponderance of the evidence)  
  * Should be significantly easier to prove than â€œbeyond a reasonable doubt.â€  
* Florida and the U.S. require â€œclear and convincing evidence.â€  
* Washington requires â€œto a reasonable certainty.â€

**Terminology**

* **Mental illness:** Medical term used by clinicians to refer to a disorder recognized by the therapeutic community for purposes of diagnosis and treatment (using DSM classifications).   
* **Insanity:** Legal term that refers to a mental state, *existing at the time of criminal offense*, considered sufficient to preclude criminal responsibility.   
* **Incompetence:** Legal term that refers to a personâ€™s mental state *at the time of a legal proceeding*. An incompetent person lacks the sufficient mental capacity to understand or participate in a relevant legal proceeding. **MPC Â§4.04**   
  * Ds can be forcibly medicated to render them competent to stand trial. (*Sell v. US*, SCOTUS 2003\) To uphold involuntary treatment, a court must find  
    * (1) The governmentâ€™s interest in trying D for the crime at issue is important;  
    * (2) Forced medication is substantially likely to render D competent and substantially unlikely to have side effects that will interfere with her ability to assist defense counsel;  
    * (3) Alternative, less intrusive treatments are unlikely to achieve substantially the same results;  
    * (4) The treatment is medically appropriate (in Dâ€™s best interest in light of her condition)

**Brief History**  
Mental hospitals started to deinstitutionalize in the 1960s with the rise of prescription drugs to treat mental illness and community clinics. Simultaneously, prisons/jails became fuller. However, there wasnâ€™t a direct relationship between deinstitutionalization and incarceration because the demographics imprisoned were not the same.Â 

Prior to the movement for deinstitutionalization, the vast majority of people institutionalized in state mental hospitals were predominantly older, white men and women. The demographic distribution of people who were incarcerated post deinstitutionalization, changed drastically and included younger, non-white people. Today, prisons and jails are approx 95% male and â…” POCs.

* Today, the largest providers of mental health services tend to be jails (**Harcourt** â†’ this is an outsourcing of mental health treatment to jails)  
  * Jail â†’ short term retention (less than a year) or pre trial detention  
  * Prison â†’ only for people who have been convicted, and sentenced generally for more than 1 year

**Insanity Defenses Tests Timeline**

* **Pre-1950** \- Insanity defense used to be controlled by Mâ€™Naghten rule â†’ dealt with cognition.  
* **1950 \- 1960** â†’ movement to expand insanity defense and add volition prong â†’ dealt with may you knew right from wrong, but you couldnâ€™t stop yourselfÂ   
* **1960s \- 1970s** â†’ period of experimentation and expansion of insanity defense in an effort to understand behavior that violates the penal code from more of mental health lensÂ   
* All this comes to an end after the *Hinckley* (attempted assassination of Reagan) trial. Hinckleyâ€™s verdict was not guilty by reason of mental insanity. Everything essentially reverted back to the Mâ€™Naghten (cognition only) standard. Some jxs got rid of insanity defense completely.Â   
* *Kahler v. U.S.* (2020) \-- SCOTUS upheld Kansasâ€™s removal of the insanity defense. If you have a mental health defense, you have to essentially funnel it through mens rea.

**Forced medication for execution:** Also, SCOTUS has never held that you canâ€™t medicate someone so that they can be forcibly medicated so they can be executed. (Potential Hippocratic oath issues)

**Prereq insanity defenses eligibility:** There must be a medical diagnosis from a psychiatrist under the DSM. 

* **TIP**: Could argue about whether or not the DSM is accurate/biased/or encompassing enough 

## MPC: INSANITY PROVISIONs {#mpc:-insanity-provisions}

**MPC Â§ 4.01 â€“ Mental Disease or Defect Excluding Responsibility**   
(1) A person is not responsible for criminal conduct if at the time of such conduct as a result of mental disease or defect he lacks substantial capacity either to appreciate the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct or to conform his conduct to the requirements of law. 

(2) â€œmental disease or defectâ€ does not include an abnormality manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct

**MPC Â§ 4.02 â€“ Mental Disease or Defect Impairing Capacity as Ground for Mitigation of Punishment in Capital Cases**

(1) Evidence of mental disease or defect is admissible if it is relevant to prove that the defendant did or didnâ€™t have a state of mind that is an element of the offense

* **Tip** \- Basically, as long as the facts also point to whether or not the defendant acted with a certain a mens rea or mental state-related aggravating/mitigating factor, then you can bring it up in your fact-pattern analysis.

**MPC Â§ 4.03 â€“ Mental Disease or Defect Excluding Responsibility is Affirmative Defense**  
(1) Mental disease of defect excluding responsibility is an affirmative defense.

**MPC Â§ 4.04 â€“ Mental Disease of Defect Excluding Fitness to Proceed \[Incompetence Provision; not Insanity\]**  
No one who lacks the capacity to understand the proceedings against him or to assist the defense attorney shall be tried, convicted or sentenced so long as he suffers the mental disease or defect.

* This is incompetence, which is slightly different from insanity. Incompetence is a legal term that refers to a personâ€™s mental state at the time of a legal proceeding. A person who lacks sufficient mental capacity to understand or participate in the relevant legal proceeding is deemed incompetent, whereas insanity is mental state at the time of the criminal conduct which can be sufficient to preclude criminal responsibility  
* **Very low bar.** All you need to understand is that the prosecution is trying to get you and the attorney is trying to help you. Very hard to win that someone is incompetent to stand trial. You will be committed until you are competent.

**Burden of Proof/Persuasion**

* About 75% states place burden on defendants (usually by preponderance of the evidence)  
  * Should be significantly easier to prove than â€œbeyond a reasonable doubt.â€  
* Florida and the U.S. require â€œclear and convincing evidence.â€  
* Washington requires â€œto a reasonable certainty.â€

## Legal Tests for Insanity {#legal-tests-for-insanity}

**EXAM TIP:** If youâ€™re arguing insanity, use MPC and Mâ€™Nagthen. Defense will argue for the MPC because its more broad so theyâ€™d be more likely to succeed under insanity defense.

Cognition ğŸ¡ª someoneâ€™s ability to understand what theyâ€™re doing  
Volition ğŸ¡ª maybe you understand right from wrong, but you couldnâ€™t stop yourself   
**N.B.** Even with all these standards, NGI acquittals are rare, even after *Hinkley.* Studies show little evidence of effect from different formulations of insanity standard. 

1. **Mâ€™Naghten rule \[cognition\]:**  
   1. Pre Req: actor is suffering from a mental disorder **AND**   
   2. (1) actor did not **know** the *nature and quality* of the act they were doing; or  
      1. Donâ€™t know what youâ€™re doing  
   3. (2) if actor did know it, they did not **know** what they were doing was **wrong**  
      1. Donâ€™t know what youâ€™re doing is wrong  
   4. ğŸ¡ª problem: too narrow. what if you know what youâ€™re doing is wrong, but canâ€™t control your acts? (think *Yates* case even though she wasnâ€™t convicted)  
   5. questions of how to interpret â€œknowâ€ and â€œwrongâ€  
      1. know \=  **narrow ğŸ¡ª** formal cognitive knowledge (can describe her acts as wrong) or **broad ğŸ¡ª** affective knowledge (emotional appreciation of wrongfulness)  
      2. wrong \= legal or moral wrong? (â€œviolating laws of both God and manâ€)  
   6. **21 states use this.**

      

2. **Irresistible Impulse Rule \[cognition \+ volition\]:**  
   1. Adds volitional prong   
      1. I.e. you might know what youâ€™re doing is wrong, but youâ€™re not able to stop yourself  
         1. Attempt to widen scope of *Mâ€™Nagthen* rule  
   2. ğŸ¡ª problem: too extreme. Requires complete destruction of mental orientation.  
        
3. **Durham Rule \[product of mental disease\]:**  
   1. Only in NH  
   2. A criminal defendant can't be convicted of a crime if the act was the product of a mental disease or defect the defendant had at the time of the incident. But defendants must prove by clear and convincing evidence
