   3. Very broad, but recognizes law will never fully understand intricacies of insanity  
   4. **Harcourt** likes this test  
   5. **Rejected and replaced by MPC; NOT USED ANYMORE. NOT IMPORTANT.**  
        
4. **MPC 4.01 \[cognition \+ volition\] \[substantial capacity\] (ALI)**  
   1. A person is not responsible for criminal conduct if, at the time of conduct, as the result of a mental disease or defect, she lacked substantial capacity (aka â€œthey had a hard timeâ€ appreciating right from wrong) to:  
      1. (1) Appreciate the â€œcriminalityâ€ (wrongfulness) of her conduct; or \[cognition\]  
         1. Criminality ğŸ¡ª more legal understanding of cognition  
         2. Wrongful ğŸ¡ª more moral understanding of cognition  
         3. ALI allowed states to use either.    
      2. (2) To conform her conduct to the requirements of the law \[volition\]  
   2. **MORE PRO DEFENDANT**

      

   3. Attempt to blend Mâ€™Naghten & irresistible impulse tests together \+ **softening**  
      1. Replaced Mâ€™Naghtenâ€™s â€œknowâ€ with â€œappreciateâ€ Ã  eliminate possibility of a formalistic interpretation  
      2. Replaced irresistible impulseâ€™s â€œtotal incapacityâ€ with â€œlacked substantial capacityâ€  
   4. **22 states use this. Substantial minority.** 

      

5. **MPC 4.04. Incompetence to stand trial, be convicted or sentenced.**  
   

   

## Mâ€™Nagtenâ€™s case (U.K., 1843\) â€“ must suffer from mental disease during commission of the act (Cognition test) {#mâ€™nagtenâ€™s-case-(u.k.,-1843)-â€“-must-suffer-from-mental-disease-during-commission-of-the-act-(cognition-test)}

## â€œto qualify for insanity defense, D must not have known that it was wrong; high standard ğŸ¡ª prosecution friendly  â€ {#â€œto-qualify-for-insanity-defense,-d-must-not-have-known-that-it-was-wrong;-high-standard-ğŸ¡ª-prosecution-friendly-â€}

**Rule**:	It must be clearly proved that, at the time of commission of the act, that the defendant suffered from a mental disease which makes him unable to know the nature and quality of the criminal conduct or the wrongfulness of the act.

* Whether the accused at the time of doing the act knew the difference between right and wrong  
  * **Harcourt:** Cognition testâ€”whether your brain understands what youâ€™re doing or right/wrong.

**Facts**: D suffers from delusions/acute insanity. He was trying to shoot the prime minister but shot his secretary instead. 

**Holding:** D will not be found guilty by reason of insanity if, as a result of mental disease:

* (1) He did not know or appreciate the nature or quality of the criminal act he committed, OR  
  * (2) He did know or appreciate the quality of the act, but he did not know that what he was doing was wrong.   
* Generally interpreted to mean total impairment: out of touch with reality, not accurately perceiving the world, hearing voices, delusional   
* This knowledge is not of the law (donâ€™t want to push this into mistake of law realm); this knowledge is of a moral wrong.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Donâ€™t forget that everyone is presumed to be sane

## Yates v. State (TX., 2005\) â€“ Issues with cognition only test; battle of experts problem {#yates-v.-state-(tx.,-2005)-â€“-issues-with-cognition-only-test;-battle-of-experts-problem}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-3}

**Facts**:	 Defendant drowned her 5 children, trying to â€œsave them from Satan.â€ There was evidence of severe depression and attempted suicide. She received psychiatric help, to whom she told about visions and voices. Yates also suffered from catatonia (physical/behavioral symptoms associated with schizophrenia). Jury rejected her insanity defense.

**TX Statute for insanity defense:** at time of the conduct charged, the actor, as a result of severe mental disease or defect, did **not know that his conduct was wrong**

* i.e. focuses very heavily on cognition (like Mâ€™Naghten): actor must not know that conduct was wrong

**Holding/Rationale:** Reversed**.**

* The lone expert testimony stating she may have gotten the story from Law & Order was false.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Takeaway:** This case shows the potential for injustice in a criminal trial that turns on a battle of the experts.   
  * This also illustrates how unpredictably a jury will find on an insanity defense.   
* **Issues with cognition only test**  
  * Here, Yates knew her conduct was forbidden b/c Satan was encouraging her to drown her children, but her will was overborne. Ultimately wasnâ€™t convicted, but still good case to show cognition only test problems.  
* Statute raises many questions  
  * 1\) Is â€œwrongâ€ a *moral* wrong or a *legal* wrong; *subjectively* wrong or â€œwrong to the worldâ€ *objectively*  
  * 2\) Is â€œknowâ€ a formal knowledge or a substantive understanding  
* Under this statute, Yates probably knew she was wrong if Satan was telling her to do it, and she wanted to be punished. However, she was still acquitted. Perhaps idea here that she was clearly insane.


## Blake v. U.S. (5th Cir., 1969\) â€“ MPC Standard: expands insanity defense to include volitional prong {#blake-v.-u.s.-(5th-cir.,-1969)-â€“-mpc-standard:-expands-insanity-defense-to-include-volitional-prong}

## â€œto use insanity defense, D must lack substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness of conduct or to conform conduct â€ {#â€œto-use-insanity-defense,-d-must-lack-substantial-capacity-to-appreciate-wrongfulness-of-conduct-or-to-conform-conduct-â€}

**Rule**:	A person is not responsible for criminal conduct if at the time of such conduct, as a result of mental disease or defect, he lacks substantial capacity either to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct (cognition prong) or to conform his conduct to the requirements of law (volition prong).

**Facts**:	 Defendant was convicted of bank robbery. He presented evidence showing that he required psychiatric treatment for at least 20 years. Expert testimony said he was schizophrenic, which often manifested in psychotic episodes. Blake was also a heavy drinker and drug user. Defense objected to *Davis* standard and proposed MPC standard instead. 

**Standards**

* *Davis* Standard (irresistible impulse): Requires that D is totally unable to distinguish right from wrong or is so mentally disoriented he cannot control his actions, such that the voluntariness of such actions is negated. OR unconscious   
  * **Elements**: (1) didnâ€™t know right vs. wrong; (2) unconscious of act; or (3) will overborne  
  * **More a matter of law (objective)**  
  * **Exam Tip: Look to 4.01 when dealing with insanity. Donâ€™t worry about *Davis* Standard.**   
* **MPC Â§4.01**:   
  * 1\) A person is not responsible for criminal conduct if at the time of such conduct as a result of mental disease or defect he lacks substantial capacity either to a) \[**cognitive prong**\] appreciate the criminality (wrongfulness) of his conduct or b) \[**volitional prong**\] to conform his conduct to the requirements of law.   
  * 2\) As used in this Article, â€œmental disease or defectâ€ do not include an abnormality manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct.   
  * **More matter of fact for jury (subjective)**

**Holding/Rationale:** *Davis* standard rejected. Substantiality type/Volitional standard re: MPC should apply. 

* Court knows that Blake suffered some mental illness under modern understandings, but *Davis* is too prohibitive/narrow.  
* *Davis* would disallow the defense whenever a person was not unconscious, incapable of distinguishing right and wrong, nor completely robbed of his free will.   
* D in this case would not win under *Davis* standard, but could under MPC incapacity standard  
  * MPC only requires **substantial lack of capacity** ğŸ¡ª more matter of fact for jury (subjective)  
  * Substantiality type leaves matter for the jury under the evidence to determine the mental disease and its relationship to Dâ€™s conduct. 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Shift towards scientific conception of mental illness:** It looks like the Court is shifting the definition of the insanity defense more towards the scientific conception of mental illness and not just a purely judge-made or statutory term.  
* **Effects of MPC Standard:** expands the kinds of psychological impairments that can excuse D, including volitional and cognitive disabilities.   
  * Does not require total impairment, standard is â€œlacks substantial capacityâ€Â â†’ may require factfinder to make a value judgment about meaning of â€œsubstantialâ€   
  * Expands scope of relevant testimony by mental health professionals   
  * â€œAppreciateâ€ rather than â€œknowâ€ suggests that Dâ€™s must have deeper understanding of the wrongfulness; broader than simple cognition   
* **Criticisms of MPC Standard**  
  * May provide too much room for experts to recognize new kinds of mental illness that can excuse individuals from criminal responsibility  
  * Mental health experts cannot determine with reasonable accuracy an individualâ€™s capacity for self-control or measure the extent of that impairment 

## U.S. v. Lyons (5th Cir., 1984\) â€“ rejection of MPC volition prong; return to cognitive test {#u.s.-v.-lyons-(5th-cir.,-1984)-â€“-rejection-of-mpc-volition-prong;-return-to-cognitive-test}

## â€œperson not responsible for criminal conduct if at the time of conduct they couldnâ€™t appreciate its wrongfulness due to mental illnessâ€ {#â€œperson-not-responsible-for-criminal-conduct-if-at-the-time-of-conduct-they-couldnâ€™t-appreciate-its-wrongfulness-due-to-mental-illnessâ€}

**Rule**:	A person is not responsible for criminal conduct on the grounds of insanity only if at the time of that conduct, as a result of a mental disease or defect, he is unable to appreciate the wrongfulness of that conduct.

**Facts**:	 Defendant was convicted of several drug crimes, which required mens reas of â€œknowingly and intentionally.â€ Lyons wanted to admit evidence pointing to his insanity, but the trial court excluded it. 

**Holding/Rationale:** Court shouldâ€™ve admitted evidence of brain and psychological damage.

* We overturn *Blake*â€™s volitional prong (lack of capacity to conform oneâ€™s conduct to the requirements of the law).

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* *Blake* to *Lyons* shows the general shift away from the MPC approach.   
  * Volitional prong of the MPC test is antiquated for several reasons.   
    * (1) a majority of psychiatrists now believe there is a lack of solid scientific bases for measuring a personâ€™s self-control capacity and for calibrating the impairment of that capacity.   
    * (2) when the court, jury, and expert witnesses engage in speculation regarding whether the defendant had the capacity to â€œcontrolâ€ himself or could have â€œresistedâ€ the criminal impulse, mistakes often result.   
    * (3) psychiatric testimony about volition is more likely to confuse jurors than psychiatric testimony about whether the defendant appreciated the wrongfulness of his act. Moreover, there is considerable overlap between a psychotic personâ€™s inability to understand and his ability to control his own behavior.   
    * (4) despite the confusion and uncertainty surrounding medical and scientific knowledge related to the inner workings of a personâ€™s mental state, federal prosecutors bear the burden of proof beyond a reasonable doubt.   
* Irresistible impulse (**minority view**): if the defendant is unable to adhere to the right as a result of irresistible impulse;  an inability to adhere to the right does not exist unless the act is committed under an irresistible impulse which completely deprives the defendant of the power of choice or volition.   
  * Must prove: (1) The existence of mental illness; and (2) that the mental illness caused the inability to control one's actions or conform one's conduct to the law.  
  * Hard and narrow

## Various Insanity Defense Reforms {#various-insanity-defense-reforms}

**Trend:** Post-*Hinckley* (attempted to assassinate Reagan ğŸ¡ª acquitted under insanity defense using MPC), there was a sharp move away from MPC and general broadening of insanity defense back to extreme *Mâ€™Naghten* test (21 states) b/c volition prong was seen as the reason why Hinckley was able to walk.

* Substantial minority of states still follow the MPC (22 states).

**Guilty But Mentally Ill**: This verdict is appropriate where D was sane at time of crime, but mentally ill at time of trial. Jury can choose among: guilty, not guilty, not guilty by reason of insanity, guilty but mentally ill.

* Effect: convicted party receives sentence that would be imposed if she were found guilty AND psychiatric care in prison setting/mental institution (criminally responsible, but mentally not responsible).  
* A minority of states adopts this.

**Abolition of insanity defense: (**1\) defense has been abused by fraudulent claims; (2) insanity only bears to grading, not conviction.

* Rebuttal: cannot get rid of insanity as defense b/c it will violate due process (but no SCOTUS cases about this; *see Clark v. Arizona where court decided there was no formalized/uniform insanity rule so formulation was open to state*), insanity defense is infrequently invoked, insanity defense is infrequently successful, and those who are successful are often committed for long periods of time  
  * Rebuttal: even w/o an insanity defense, thereâ€™s still the req. of voluntary act, so due process will not be violated.

| Expanding Liability |
| :---: |

**Traditional methods of expanding liability beyond the completed crime (inchoate crimes):**

|  | Attempt Charged for a different crime â€œattempted murderâ€ | Complicity Charged for same crime: accomplice to murder \= murder | Conspiracy  2 crimes: murder \+ conspiracy to murder |
| :---- | :---- | :---- | :---- |
| **Mens Rea** | What intent is required? | **Actus Reus** | What does D need to have done? Possibilities:  First step Last step Substantial step â€“ 5.01(2) Withdrawal  | How much do you have to aid/abet to be complicit? Must aid or abet; mere presence is not enough  | 1\) Agreement to conspire (*Interstate Circuit â€“* can infer agreement) 2\) Overt act requirement â€“ 5.03(5) Must be in furtherance of conspiracy to show that its gone beyond the purely mental state and has reached the implementation stage Crime doesnâ€™t need to be completed Doesnâ€™t merge into the crime, if completed. Still two separate charges |
| **Punishment**  | How much do you punish? Separate crime at Â½ grade below or Same punishment (MPC: considers an unsuccessful criminal just as dangerous as a successful one)  | How much do you punish? Same as principal  Accessory after the crime is subject to less punishment  | Separate offense, so two convictions (1 for doing the crime, 1 for conspiring to commit the crime) Lots of benefits for prosecution. Fixed term (5 years) or tied to object crime |
| **Scope of Liability** | N/A | Natural and probable consequences of conduct that A set in motion (*Luparello â€“* min. rule. Rejected by MPC). See how you wonâ€™t need to go through conspiracy and Pinkerton rule here. Must be within Aâ€™s conscious objectives; cannot be wholly separate crime (MPC comment to 2.06) |  |
| **Affirmative Defense** | Renunciation: actor abandoned effort to commit the crime or otherwise prevented its commission, under circumstances manifesting a **complete and voluntary** renunciation of his criminal purpose. MPC 5.01(4) N.B. This defense doesnâ€™t affect the liability of an accomplice who didnâ€™t join in abandonment or prevention. Impossibility:  Factual impossibility ğŸ¡ª no defense.  Eg: you tried to pickpocket someone, but were unsuccessful because they didnâ€™t have a wallet.  Legal impossibility ğŸ¡ª a valid defense. eg: statutory rape, but victim is actually above age. A VALID DEFENSE.  | Actor is a victim of the offense â€“ 2.06(6)(a) Defined Offense: the offense is so defined that his conduct is inevitably incident to its commission â€“ 2.06(6)(b)  Termination prior to commission of offense MPC 2.06(6)(c): Actor must **wholly deprive** it of effectiveness in the commission of the offense or give timely warning to law enforcement authorities or otherwise make proper effort to prevent the commission of the offense  | Renunciation of Criminal Purpose: After conspiring to commit a crime, the actor thwarted the success of the conspiracy, under circumstances manifest a complete and voluntary renunciation of his criminal purpose. MPC 5.03(6) |

* **Attempt:** Inchoate (preparatory to further a criminal act) crimes impose liability before the harm intended fully occurs.   
  * Gets at a crime that has not been committed i.e. the result is not achieved, but the D can be convicted for attempting to commit the crime.  
* **Complicity** merges into the principal crime, so no different crime: the perpetrator would not be indicted for complicity, but rather the crime she abetted or solicited. Thus, complicity is a method for making someone criminally liable.  
* **Conspiracy** represents a separate crime. The perpetrator can be charged on both offenses: one for the crime and the other for conspiracy to commit the crime.

# Attempt {#attempt}

| Common Law | MPC |
| :---- | :---- |
| Specific intent (even if target crime has lesser mens rea. Ex. Murder satisfied w/knowledge mens rea, but attempt to murder requires specific intent to kill.) | Same mens rea as target crime Result: purpose OR belief Att. Circumstance: mens rea of target crime |
| Focuses on what D has left to be done (preparatory acts less probative) | Focuses on what D has done (preparatory acts more probative) |
| Reduced punishment from target crime | Same punishment as target crime |

**Common Law Attempt**

* Attempt is a specific intent crime.  
* **Mens and Actus Reus**  
  * Actor had specific intent to commit a crime but failed \[mens rea\]  
  * Actor took acts beyond mere preparation toward commission of the offense \[actus reus\]  
* Rizzo dangerous proximity test (people v. Rizzo)

Not a separate action. If actor completed crime cannot be also convicted of attempt (unlike conspiracy).

**Theoretical justifications for specific intent req.** 

* **Retribution:** not blameworthy if reckless   
* **Utilitarian:** no deterrence b/c no intent

**Theoretical justifications for not punishing preparation:**

* Retribution: Autonomy. Maximizes opportunity to repent.  
* Utilitarian: No deterrence value in punishing someone who hasnâ€™t actually committed a crime.

**Two types of attempt cases** 

1. Crimes where the person does a lot, but doesnâ€™t commit the final act (*Rizzo*)  
2. Failed attempts ğŸ¡ª person took all the steps, but the crime doesnâ€™t occur (eg: you plant a bomb on the plane, but it doesnâ€™t go off.

**MPC Â§ 5.01 â€“ Criminal Attempt**  
(1) A person is guilty of attempt if, acting with the kind of culpability otherwise required for commission of the crime, he:

* (a) purposely engages in conduct which would constitute the crime if the attendant circumstances were as he believes them to be; **CONDUCT CRIME**  
  * Purpose: highest MR for the conduct itself. Belief for the AC. Cf. 2.02(4)  
      
* (b) when causing a particular result is an element of the crime, does or omits to do anything with the purpose of causing or with the belief that it will cause such result without further conduct on his part; OR **RESULT**  
  * **Tip:** Purpose: you need to intend the result to occur. You can also go in under â€œbelief without further conductâ€ part. Takes out down-the-line causes that bring about the result. If you go under belief, the act/omission must be the single cause of the result, which more direct than the â€œpurposeâ€ prong.   
      
* (c) purposely does or omits to do anything which, under the circumstances as he believes them to be, is an act or omission constituting a substantial step in a course of conduct planned to culminate in his commission of the crime. **CONDUCT â€“ SUBSTANTIAL STEP**  
  * Substantial step to execute a plan. This is important.

(2) **Substantial step** under (1)(c) requires â€œstrongly corroborative of the actorâ€™s criminal purpose.â€ The following may prove a substantial step:

* (a) lying in waiting, searching for, or following the contemplated victim  
* (b) enticing or seeking to entice the contemplated victim of the crime to go to the place contemplated for its commission  
* (c) reconnoitering the place contemplated for the commission of the crime  
* (d) unlawful entry of a structure, vehicle or enclosure in which it is contemplated that the crime will be  
* committed;  
* (e) possession of materials to be used for committing the crime, which are specially designed for such use or which serve no lawful purpose under the circumstances  
* (f) possession, collection, or fabrication of materials to be used in the crime, at or near contemplated place of crime, where such possession, collection, or fabrication serves no lawful purpose under the circumstances  
* (g) soliciting an innocent agent to engage in conduct constituting an element of crime

N.B. â€“ (e) are physical acts by the defendant, whereas (f) is (conduct through words) to get another person to carry out the crime.  But (f) is more specific as to the actual crime. 

* Maybe a sliding scale of generality v. specificity / physical acts v. conduct through words / ultimate conduct by defendant v. ultimate conduct by an agent

(3) Engaging in conduct that would render you complicit under MPC Â§ 2.06 if the crime were committed, is guilty of attempt, even though the crime is not committed or attempted by the principal criminal actor. **Attempted Complicity.** 

* This is attempted complicity. It seems to impose liability on preparing for a crime, because it says â€œeven though the crime is not committed or attempted by the other person.   
  * So far removed from the actual result or actual conduct of the crime, that it appears to cover a lot of conduct.

(4) It is an **affirmative defense** that the actor abandoned the criminal effort or otherwise prevented its commission, under circumstances manifesting a complete and voluntary renunciation of his criminal purpose. This defense doesnâ€™t affect the liability of an accomplice who didnâ€™t join in abandonment or prevention.

* Just because you abandon doesnâ€™t mean I get the same defense  
  * **One-way street**: Liability flows from principal to accomplice; but defense doesnâ€™t flow from principal to accomplice

**DIFFICULT ISSUE**: Can an actor be guilty of attempted reckless homicide when actor was acting *recklessly* (ie. without intent)?

1. Under common law this is very difficult b/c you need a specific intent to murder. Recklessness requires that even though you were *aware* of a substantial risk, you ignored it rather than intended it.

2. Also difficult under MPC b/c MPC still requires purpose to commit crime or knowledge criminal result will follow. Knowledge requires awareness that result will *certainly* follow; whereas, recklessness is just awareness that result is a *substantial possibility.*

 


## Smallwood v. State (MD., 1996\) â€“ attempt requires specific intent (HIV Spreader) {#smallwood-v.-state-(md.,-1996)-â€“-attempt-requires-specific-intent-(hiv-spreader)}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-4}

**Rule**:	An intent to kill may be permissibly inferred by the trier of fact if the defendantâ€™s natural and probable acts, conduct, and words directly led to the death of the victim.

* An attempt requires a purpose (â€œspecific intentâ€) to produce the proscribed result, even when recklessness or some lesser mens rea would suffice for conviction of the completed offense.

  **Facts**:	D, HIV-positive rapist, did not use condoms in any of his attacks, exposing his victims to risk of HIV infection and resulting risk of death by AIDS. The prosecution analogized the defendant carrying HIV to carrying a deadly weapon and deploying it through unprotected sex.

**Holding/Rationale:** D should not be convicted for attempted murder b/c he did not have a specific intent to kill (no mens rea).

* Ds raping women w/knowledge that he was HIV positive does NOT constitute specific intent to kill b/c magnitude of risk of death is not certain enough (prob of death by AIDS \< prob of death by gun). Probability only reaches to reckless homicide.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Use of circumstantial evidence to prove intent:** Intent to kill may be proved by circumstantial evidence such as acts, conduct and words, including 1\) explicit statements demonstrating an intent to infect their victims or 2\) taking specific actions demonstrating such an intent and tending to exclude other possible intents.   
  * Ex: D jabbed syringe into Vâ€™s arm while shouting â€œIâ€™ll give you AIDSâ€ (Caine)  
  * Ex: D fired weapon directly at a vital part of the human body (Raines)  
  * N.B. evidence must be proximately related.  
* **POLICY:** Should we punish based on intent or result?  
  * **Utilitarian**: result to deter such behavior (general deterrence)  
  * **Retributivist**: intent b/c only want to punish blameworthy behavior

## People v. Rizzo (NY., 1927\) â€“ mere preparation not enough to constitute attempt \[dangerous proximity test\] (rob payroll man) {#people-v.-rizzo-(ny.,-1927)-â€“-mere-preparation-not-enough-to-constitute-attempt-[dangerous-proximity-test]-(rob-payroll-man)}

## â€œdangerous proximity test: attempt must come immediately near to completing the intended crimeâ€ {#â€œdangerous-proximity-test:-attempt-must-come-immediately-near-to-completing-the-intended-crimeâ€}

**Rule**:	To be convicted of attempt, the acts tending towards the commission of a crime must be so near to its accomplishment that in all reasonable probability the crime itself wouldâ€™ve been committed, but for timely interference.

**Facts**:	Defendant was with a group planning to rob Charles Rao while on his way to cash his paycheck. Rizzo and the crew drove around looking for their target, but they never found him. Police arrested them before they could rob Rao.

**Holding/Rationale:** Steps taken by defendant (driving around looking for V) are not sufficient to constitute attempt to commit robbery. They are merely preparation. 

* The law doesnâ€™t recognize mere preparation tending towards the commission of a crime.  
* The acts tending towards the commission of a crime must be so near to its accomplishment that in all reasonable probability the crime itself wouldâ€™ve been committed, but for timely interference.  
* There must be dangerous proximity to success (*Hyde*).

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* But what about MPC 5.01(2)(a) lying in waiting, searching for, or following the victim? Under MPC, D wouldâ€™ve been guilty.  
  * This is pre-MPC, so it might not be that relevant for our purposes. But you have a very defendant-friendly holding relative to the MPC.  
* **Harcourt:** doesnâ€™t like this test because extraordinary protective of Ds. Probably shouldâ€™ve found attempt here. 

## McQuirter v. State (AL., 1953\) â€“ black man allegedly follows white woman down the street {#mcquirter-v.-state-(al.,-1953)-â€“-black-man-allegedly-follows-white-woman-down-the-street}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-5}

**Rule**:	Whether a person has the intention of committing a criminal act at the time of an attempt can be found based on the facts and circumstances presented at trial.

**Facts**:	 A Black man was found guilty of attempted assault with intent to rape. Allegedly, he followed a white woman with her children into a white neighborhood. When he approached her, she told her children to run and get Simmons. McQuirter left after seeing Simmons. The police chief testified that McQuirter confessed to an intent to rape the first woman to pass his truck. McQuirter testified that he was simply sitting in the area where the woman was.

**Holding/Rationale:** There is enough evidence to affirm that D is guilty of attempt to commit an assault with intent to rape. 

* To justify a conviction for an attempt to commit an assault with intent to rape, jury must find beyond a reasonable doubt that D intended to have sex with V against her will, by force or by putting her in fear.   
* Intent is to be determined from facts and circumstances adduced on trial.  
  * â€œJury may consider social conditions and customs founded upon racial differences, such as that V was a white woman and D was a black manâ€   
* **Result:** Only evidence of culpable intent was sheriffâ€™s claims of Ds confession and Ds conviction upheld.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Projecting racial prejudice into mens rea for attempt. Court essentially said you can infer the mens rea based on race.  
* **TIP:** Good case to bring up when talking about criminal law & race with *McCleskey, Goetz (previous robberies),* skew in incarceration, racial profiling cases.  
* **POLICY/Theoretical Readings**  
  * \*Harcourt, Post-Modern Meditations. Reason is just a front for social customs/beliefs of those in power. Only dominant social conditions and customs of ruling class are considered.

# Complicity {#complicity}

Aiding and abetting or accomplice liability. **Charged for same crime: accomplice to murder \= murder**.   
**To be an accomplice, principal MUST complete the act.** MPC 2.06(1)

**Main issue**: **MENS REA.** Easy to satisfy â€œaid and abetâ€ (actus reus), but prosecution needs to consider whether the act was significant enough to warrant accomplice liability b/c it amounts to liability for the full crime.

**Common law** had many types of accomplices, but they are mostly done away with except for accessory after the fact.

**MPC Â§ 2.06: Liability for Conduct of Another: Complicity**   
(1) A person is guilty of an offense if, it is committed by his own conduct or by the conduct of another person for which he is legally accountable, or both.

(2) A person is legally accountable for the conduct of another person when:

* (a) acting with the requisite mens rea for the offense, he causes an innocent or irresponsible person to engage in such conduct; OR  
  * **Note**: Notice that â€œcauseâ€ is broader than â€œsolicit to engageâ€ in MPC 5.01(2)(g) \- **attempt**. Thereâ€™s also no limiting standard like â€œreasonably foreseeableâ€ or even â€œbut-forâ€ on the face of the statute. Those might be read in, but all you need is a cause (no matter how remote or insignificant)  
* (b) he is made accountable for the conduct of such other person by the Code or the law defining the offense; OR  
  * **Tip:** Just look to the attached statutes on the exam to see if there are any defined relationships that make you legally accountable for anotherâ€™s actions (coercion, spousal, employer-employee)  
* (c) he is an accomplice of such other person in the commission of the offense. **Defined in (3)**

3\) A person is an accomplice if: **CONDUCT CRIMES**

* (a) with the purpose of promoting or facilitating the commission of the offense, he:  
  * (i) **solicits** such other person to commit it; OR  
    * Words or conduct (eg: some do this thing with me)   
  * (ii) **aids, or agrees or attempts to aid** such other person in planning or committing it; OR  
    * Very broad coverage bc it says â€œagrees or attempts to aidâ€ in planning  
      * Like you can â€œagreeâ€ to plan and not do it and still potentially be charged  
  * (iii) having a **legal duty** to prevent the commission of the offense, fails to make proper effort so to do; OR  
    * Where would you have a duty to rescue? Maybe psychiatrist? Law enforcement?  
* (b) his conduct is expressly declared by law to establish his complicity

**Being an accomplice is quite easy to be, but remember that under (1), the principal still needs to commit the act**. That preserves the actus reus requirement for the principal crime.

(4) If causing a result is an element of the offense, an accomplice to that crime is guilty if he acts with the requisite mens rea, if any, with respect to that result that is sufficient for the commission of the offense. **RESULT CRIMES.**

* Accompliceâ€™s MR is the same as the MR for the underlying crime. This concept is exemplified in *Roebuck*, which required only a MR of malice (nebulous), and *McVay* (crim neg)

(5) A person who is legally incapable of committing a particular offense may be guilty if it is committed by the conduct of another for which he is legally accountable, unless such liability is inconsistent with the purpose of the provision establishing incapacity.

* Examples of legal incapability ğŸ¡ª Insanity, age, diplomatic immunity  
* **TIP:** Look for an explicit provision talking about complicity (clear statement); can still be liable for complicity unless the purpose of the law is clear that complicity is off the table

(6) ) Unless otherwise provided by the Code or by the law defining the offense, a person is not an accomplice in an offense committed by another person if:

* (a) he is a victim of that offense; or   
* (b) the offense is so defined that his conduct is inevitably incident to its commission; or   
* (c) he terminates his complicity prior to the commission of the offense and   
  * (i) wholly deprives it of effectiveness in the commission of the offense; or   
  * (ii) gives timely warning to the law enforcement authorities or otherwise makes proper effort to prevent the commission of the offense.

(7) An accomplice may be convicted of a crime even if the principal isnâ€™t convicted or is convicted of a different crime. (not the exact words)

## Mens Rea for Conduct, Result and Attendant Circumstances {#mens-rea-for-conduct,-result-and-attendant-circumstances}

**Harcourt:** you need to not only know what the mens rea level is (purpose, knowledge, reckless), BUT also what the object of the mens rea is (i.e. does it need to be directed at the principalâ€™s or about the crime itself)

* **Conduct**: For accomplice liability, accomplice must have specific intent (purpose) to further the underlying conduct   
* **Result**: For accomplice liability, accomplice need only have the mens rea required for the result element of the substantive offense   
  * **Contrast with Attempt**: MPC is more demanding in attempt (specific intent/belief will result, even when recklessness or negligence would suffice) than complicity (same MR as principal).  
* **Attendant Circumstances**: MPC is intentionally silent on this; attitude towards circumstances is left to resolution by the courts   
  * **Contrast with Attempt:** MPC is less demanding in attempt (same as completed) than complicity (silent, left for courts to decide)

| ATTEMPT v. ACCOMPLICE MENS REA UNDER MPC |  |  |
| ----- | :---- | :---- |
|  | **Attempt** | **Accomplice** |
| Conduct | Purpose | Purpose (specific intent) |
| Result | Purpose/belief	(even	if	completed offense has lesser mens rea) | Same mens rea as underlying crime |
| Att. Circumstances | Same as underlying offense | Open to court discretion |
| **N.B.** This is why generally easier to convict someone under accomplice liability than attempt\! Although, could be harder if court says you need purpose/knowledge of attendant circumstances. |  |  |

## Attempt as an accomplice {#attempt-as-an-accomplice}

You can be charged as an accomplice for *attempting* to aid under 2.06(3)(a)(ii). This is a provision for conduct crimes. Mens rea \= purpose. 

**MPC: 2.06 Liability for Conduct of Another: Complicity**   
3\) A person is an accomplice if:

* (a) with the purpose of promoting or facilitating the commission of the offense, he:  
  * (ii) aids, or agrees or attempts to aid (AR) such other person in planning or committing it; OR

## Hicks v. U.S. (1983) â€“ d must intend to aid in/encourage crime to be convicted of complicity; presence not enough unless evidence of prior conspiracy {#hicks-v.-u.s.-(1983)-â€“-d-must-intend-to-aid-in/encourage-crime-to-be-convicted-of-complicity;-presence-not-enough-unless-evidence-of-prior-conspiracy}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-6}

**Rule**:	**Complicity:** A person who is present at the scene of a crime committed by someone else, and who says or does something that encourages the other person to commit the crime, is not guilty as an accessory to a crime unless the person intended his or her words or acts to aid and abet.

* **Conspiracy:** A person who previously conspired with another to commit a crime is guilty as an accessory if he was present when the other person committed the crime, even if he did nothing at the scene to aid or abet.  
    
  **Facts**:	Defendant was jointly indicted with Rowe for the murder of Colvard. Witnesses said that during the incident, Hicks laughed, telling Colvard to take his hat off and â€œdie like a man.â€ Rowe shot Colvard. Rowe and Hicks rode off together. Hicks testified that he didnâ€™t intend to encourage Rowe to kill Colvard.


**Holding/Rationale:** A defendant canâ€™t be an accomplice to murder for uttering words that had the effect of encouraging murder, even though the defendant didnâ€™t intend it. **A defendant needs to intend to aid in/encourage the murder.**

* No evidence that Hicks wouldâ€™ve committed the crime had Rowe not been there, so presence is insufficient to establish intent.  
* If your only evidence is words, you need intent to aid/encourage the crime or some facts pointing to conspiracy 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **MR:** A true purpose, often called a specific intent, is generally required to hold a person liable as an accomplice; that is, he must actually intend his action to further the criminal *action* of the principal.  
* Court also mentioned that if there was evidence of a previous conspiracy between Hicks and Rowe to kill Colvard, Hicks would be guilty

## State v. Gladstone (WA., 1970\) â€“ conduct crime mens rea (purpose) {#state-v.-gladstone-(wa.,-1970)-â€“-conduct-crime-mens-rea-(purpose)}

## â€œ*The aider/abettor must do something in association or connection w/ principal to accomplish the crime*â€ {#â€œthe-aider/abettor-must-do-something-in-association-or-connection-w/-principal-to-accomplish-the-crimeâ€}

**Rule**:	**Conduct crime:** Mere communications to the effect that another might/or probably would commit a criminal offense does not amount to aiding and abetting of the offense. Accomplice must wish to bring out and have purposive attitude in his action to make the principalâ€™s act succeed.

**Facts**:	Defendants were all students. Police hired Thompson as an informant to buy pot from Gladstone, but he didnâ€™t have any. Gladstone gave him Kentâ€™s information to buy pot. No evidence of communication between Gladstone and Kent concerning the sale of pot. Gladstone charged with aiding and abetting the sale.

**Holding/Rationale:** A defendant canâ€™t be guilty of aiding and abetting where there is no connection/association with the principal to accomplish the crime.

* Gladstone may have helped Thompson purchase weed, but he didnâ€™t help Kent sell it.  
  * There was no prior contact (nexus) between G and K, only G and T  
* You need some sort of association with the venture, participation in the conduct, and intent for the criminal conduct to succeed.  
* Mere communication to the effect that someone might commit a crime is insufficient.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Harcourt**: Intuitions about standard of care (weed v. kill)  
* **What if under MPC**: Marijuana sale is treated as a conduct crime, so Â§2.06(3)(a)(ii)): G must have â€œpurpose of promoting or facilitatingâ€ Kâ€™s sale of weed (MR) and must â€œaid or agree or attempt to aidâ€ K in planning or committing it (AR).  
* **Hypos**  
  * Notes: (1) G and K living together and G tells T itâ€™s good stuff (liable); G present at the transaction (liable)  
  * **Harcourt**: What if T says â€œI want to kill someone; do you know any hitman?â€ And G gave K address and drew map.

## State v. McVay (RI., 1926\) â€“ Mens Rea for result crime: result doesnâ€™t needed to be intended (steamer ship explosion) {#state-v.-mcvay-(ri.,-1926)-â€“-mens-rea-for-result-crime:-result-doesnâ€™t-needed-to-be-intended-(steamer-ship-explosion)}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-7}

**Rule**:	The mens rea required for accessory in a result-based crime is only the intent to act, not the intent to cause the result. 

* Complicity requires specific intent to aid principalâ€™s conduct, but does not require result of conduct to be intended (D may be an accomplice in a crime he has no intention of committing); same standard as MPC  
* **Contrast w/conduct crimes:** Accompliceâ€™s must have **specific intent (purpose)** regarding **principalâ€™s conduct**, but for **result,** he only needs mens rea necessary for resulting crime.  
  		  
  **Facts**:	A steamerâ€™s boiler exploded and killed people. Captain was indicted for criminal manslaughter. D, person who hired Captain, was indicted as an accessory before the fact for aiding and abetting the Captain by intentionally directing and counseling the grossly negligent act of maintaining an unsafe boiler, with full knowledge of the possible danger to human life. 

**Holding/Rationale:** The indictment against Kelley (D) can stand.

* Criminally negligent manslaughter means killing without premeditation, deliberation or intent to kill or commit a felony which may reasonably be expected to result in death/serious bodily injury. It is criminally negligent in that it is characterized by reckless disregard for the consequences of oneâ€™s actions or indifference to the safety of others.   
  * Here, D, with full knowledge of the possible danger to human life, recklessly and willfully advised, counseled and commanded the Captain to take the chance of maintaining the unsafe boiler, which resulted in the crime (criminally negligent manslaughter).   
* Kelley need not have intended the result of death, only that he intentionally directed and counseled the grossly negligent act, which the indictment charges resulted in the crime.  
* The mens rea required for accessory in a result-based crime is only the intent to act, not the intent to cause the result.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Manslaughter in RI requires criminal negligence, so Kelley needed to have been criminally negligent as to the result, but intentional about the counseling and procuring. 2.06(4)  
* **More defendant friendly bc it doesnâ€™t hinge on a nature and probable consequence doctrine. SAME WITH ROEBUCK**

## Commonwealth v. Roebuck (PA., 2011\) â€“ mens rea for result crime \= mens rea req. of principal for offense {#commonwealth-v.-roebuck-(pa.,-2011)-â€“-mens-rea-for-result-crime-=-mens-rea-req.-of-principal-for-offense}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-8}

**Rule**:	An accomplice can be convicted for murder if the accomplice acted with the culpable mental state required of the principal actor, and intentionally aids the principal actor in the commission of the crime.

**Facts**:	V was lured to an apartment complex, where he was ambushed and shot. D participated with others in orchestrating the events but did not shoot the victim. D claimed there could be no accomplice liability for third-degree murder (an unintentional killing) because people canâ€™t intend to aid an unintentional act. Â 

**Holding/Rationale:** Conviction stands; accomplice liability doesnâ€™t require that the defendant had the conscious objective to help cause a particular result.

* The defendant only needs to intend to aid the conduct, which unfortunately, results in death.  
* With respect to the result (death), the defendant still needs to be reckless (or whatever other mens rea).

**Concurrence:** Accomplice does not intend to aid an unintentional murder; he intends to aid a malicious act which results in a killing  
**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* For complicity, the required mens rea for result crimes is the same as the mens rea required for commission of the offense (// MPC) \*specific intent to aid a reckless crime found, where accompliceâ€™s mens rea was recklessness. MPC 2.06(4)  
* **More defendant friendly bc it doesnâ€™t doesnâ€™t hinge on a nature and probable consequence doctrine. SAME WITH MCVAY**

## People v. Luparello (CA., 1987\) â€“ nature and probably consequences theory: scope of accomplice liability. MIN RULE. {#people-v.-luparello-(ca.,-1987)-â€“-nature-and-probably-consequences-theory:-scope-of-accomplice-liability.-min-rule.}

## â€œ*accomplice liable for all natural and probable (foreseeable) consequences of principal (rejected by MPC)*â€ {#â€œaccomplice-liable-for-all-natural-and-probable-(foreseeable)-consequences-of-principal-(rejected-by-mpc)â€}

**Rule**:	D may be found guilty not only of the offense he intended to facilitate/encourage, but also of any reasonably foreseeable offense committed by the person he aids and abets.

**Facts**:	D was distraught after his ex-gf married someone else. D called on his friends to find Martin (V) and get info about her whereabouts at any cost. Friends visited Martin, who wouldnâ€™t say anything. The next night, the friends ambushed Martin and killed him (D wasnâ€™t there). Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder on a theory of accomplice liability.

**Holding/Rationale:** A person can be liable for the unplanned and unintended acts of co-conspirators that are the natural, probable, and foreseeable consequences of his assistance.

* Similar to the proximate cause inquiry (reasonably foreseeable).  
  * The fact that the accomplice or co-conspirator intended to facilitate some less serious criminal act doesnâ€™t render the foreseeable consequence doctrine inapplicable.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* You have specific intent to promote/encourage getting the whereabouts of the ex â€œat any costâ€   
* Liability extends to reach the actual crime, rather than the planned/intended crime.   
  * **POLICY:** Accomplices should be responsible for the criminal harms they have naturally and foreseeably put in motion. (basically *Pinkerton* liability, but for complicity)  
* MPC rejects natural and probable consequences doctrine for accomplice liability. See MPC 2.06 Comment. 

## Roy v. U.S. (D.C., 1995\) â€“ naturable and probable consequences doctrine limited to crimes occuring in the ordinary course of things {#roy-v.-u.s.-(d.c.,-1995)-â€“-naturable-and-probable-consequences-doctrine-limited-to-crimes-occuring-in-the-ordinary-course-of-things}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-9}

**Rule**:	Under the natural and probable consequences doctrine, a defendant may not be convicted as an aider and abettor of a crime that would not be expected to occur in the ordinary course of things.

**Facts**:	Miller, a confidential informant approached Roy to buy a handgun. Roy referred Miller to Ross. The gun and money exchanged hands, but Ross ended up just robbing Miller of the money and taking the gun. Trial court instructed jury that Roy could be liable for armed robbery if the robbery was the natural and probable consequence of the illegal attempt to sell a handgun.

**Holding/Rationale:** Reversed; insufficient evidence to show intent to participate in robbery

* Roy intended an illegal sale, not a robbery.   
* This would expand liability too much, as Roy would go from a misdemeanor to felony punishable by life imprisonment.   
* It is not enough to show that the accomplice knew or shouldâ€™ve known that the principal might conceivably commit the offense the accomplice is charged with aiding and abetting.  
* Natural and probable in the ordinary course of things presupposes an outcome within a reasonably predictable range.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Contrast with Luparello** â€“ this case is more defendant favorable.   
  * This seems to be more forgiving to defendants than *Luparello*  
  * Hereâ€™s a reversal of conviction whereas *Luparello* affirmed conviction

## Different Ways To InTerpret Accomplice MR {#different-ways-to-interpret-accomplice-mr}

![][image8]

1. D has MR as to Crime B  
   1. Accompliceâ€™s MR is sufficient for the crime   
      2. NY Penal Law: â€œacting with the mental culpability required for the commission thereof, he solicits, requires, commands, importunes or intentionally aids such person to engage in such conduct.â€   
      3. MPC Â§2.06(4) Result crime MR   
   2. D has MR as to â€œaidingâ€ principal  
      1. Purpose is to help the principal do what principal wants to do   
         1. Related to the crime   
   3. D has MR as to principalâ€™s MR  
      1. D knows principalâ€™s MR, knows P is doing something bad  
      2. CA Standard Jury Instructions: â€œwith the knowledge of the unlawful purpose of perpetrator of the crimeâ€   
   4. D has MR as to principalâ€™s MR & AR  
      1. Accomplice knows principalâ€™s MR & AR  
      2. IL Statute: â€œwith the intent to promote or facilitate such commissionâ€   
         1. Intent is directed towards the commission of the crime   
      3. MPC Â§2.06(3): â€œwith the purpose of promoting or facilitating the commission of the offenseâ€   
   5. Principal has MR as to Crime B   
      1. Typical culpability 

# Conspiracy {#conspiracy}

Sets the threshold of criminality much earlier than attempt 

## MPC: Conspiracy {#mpc:-conspiracy}

**MPC Â§ 5.03 â€“ Criminal Conspiracy**   
(1) **Definition of Conspiracy.** A person is guilty of conspiracy with another person or persons to commit a crime if with the purpose **(MR)** of promoting or facilitating its commission he:

* (a) agrees with **(AR)** such other person or persons that they or one or more of them will engage in conduct which constitutes such crime or an attempt or solicitation to commit such crime; OR  
* (b) agrees to aid **(AR)** such other person or persons in the planning or commission of such crime or of an attempt or solicitation to commit such crime.

(2) **Scope of Conspiratorial Relationship.** If a person guilty of conspiracy, as defined by (1), knows that a person with whom he conspires to commit a crime has conspired with another person or persons to commit the same crime, he is guilty of conspiring with such other person or persons, whether or not he knows their identity, to commit such crime.

(3) **Conspiracy with Multiple Criminal Objectives.** If a person conspires to commit a number of crimes, he is guilty of only one conspiracy so long as such multiple crimes are the object of the same agreement or continuous conspiratorial relationship.

(4) **Joinder and Venue in Conspiracy Prosecutions.** 

(5) **Overt Act.** No person may be convicted of conspiracy to commit a crime, other than a felony of the first or second degree, unless an overt act in pursuance of such conspiracy is alleged and proved to have been done by him or by a person with whom he conspired.

(6) **Renunciation of Criminal Purpose.** It is an affirmative defense that the actor, after conspiring to commit a crime, thwarted the success of the conspiracy, under circumstances manifesting a complete and voluntary renunciation of his criminal purpose.

(7) **Duration of the conspiracy (favorable to prosecution)** 

* (a) conspiracy is a continuing course of conduct which terminates when the crime or crimes which are its object ***are committed*** or the agreement that they be committed ***is abandoned*** by the defendant and by those with whom he conspired; AND  
* (b) such abandonment is presumed if neither the defendant nor anyone with whom he conspired does any ***overt act*** in pursuance of the conspiracy during the applicable period of limitation; AND  
* (c) if an ***individual*** abandons the agreement, the conspiracy is terminated as to him only if and when he ***advises*** those with whom he conspired of his abandonment or he ***informs the law enforcement*** authorities of the existence of the conspiracy and of his participation therein.

## Prosecutorial advantages (*Krulewitch)* {#prosecutorial-advantages-(krulewitch)}

**Prosecutorial Advantages** (*Krulewitch*)

* Hearsay evidence can be admitted (as long as itâ€™s in furtherance of the conspiracy)   
* Bootstrapping: can use hearsay to prove conspiracy, which then allows you to bring in hearsay (very circular)  
* Joint trials  
  * Accumulates the evidence, can influence jury  
  * Allows the prosecutor to create a feeling of birds flocking together  
* Location of trial  
  * More flexible, can forum/venue shop  
  * Prosecutor allowed to venue shop (many options â€“ where the Ds are, where the agreements were made, where the overt acts occurred)   
* Statute of limitations  
  * Starts to run when last act of conspiracy is completed, but conspiracies are usually ongoing, so there are very few SOL limitations   
  * Generally longer SOLs because conspiracy end dates are more nebulous   
* Cumulative punishment  
  * Being punished for conspiracy and underlying crime   
  * MPC tries to avoid this by barring cumulative penalties for substantive object crimes (with some exceptions)  
* *Pinkerton* rule (foreseeability. **Minority rule**)

## Krulewitch v. U.S. (1949 â€“ Prosecutorial advantages for the state when bringing a conspiracy charge {#krulewitch-v.-u.s.-(1949-â€“-prosecutorial-advantages-for-the-state-when-bringing-a-conspiracy-charge}

## *â€œ* â€ {#â€œ-â€}

**Rule**:	A conspiracy is considered so vagrant as to have been committed in any district where any one of the conspirators did any of the acts, however, innocent, intended to accomplish its object.

* Once the trial begins, the defendant is bombarded with evidence of acts of others he may not have authorized, intended, or known about, all of which is admissible to the existence of a conspiracy.  
* If the defendant is silent, he is taken to admit the conspiracy and if co-defendants turn on each other, they convict each other.


**Takeaway:** *Krulewitch* demonstrates the procedural advantages for the government when bringing a conspiracy charge.

## Actus REus: Conspiracy  {#actus-reus:-conspiracy}

**Actus Reus**

* Agreement \+ Overt Act   
* Agreement ğŸ¡ª 5.03(1)(a) or (b)  
  * Not necessary to prove an express agreement; circumstantial/inferential proof is sufficient (*Interstate* â€“ agreement can be inferred)   
  * Do not need to know every single detail of the conspiracy or all the details of the conspiracy   
* Overt Act ğŸ¡ª 5.03(5)  
  * An act in furtherance of the conspiracy, demonstrating that the act has gone beyond the purely mental state and has reached implementation stage   
  * Under common law, no overt act required   
  * Under MPC, overt act required if substantive offense is relatively minor â†’ pretty low bar, any act will suffice   
    * No overt act required if substantive offense is serious (agreement is the actus reus) 

## Interstate circuit v. U.S. (1939) â€“agreements may be inferred based on the circumstances (1 part of actus reus) {#interstate-circuit-v.-u.s.-(1939)-â€“agreements-may-be-inferred-based-on-the-circumstances-(1-part-of-actus-reus)}

## â€œexpress agreements not necessary; agreement can be inferred based on circumstantial evidenceâ€ {#â€œexpress-agreements-not-necessary;-agreement-can-be-inferred-based-on-circumstantial-evidenceâ€}

**Rule**:	Express agreement is not necessary; agreement can be inferred/based on circumstantial evidence

**Facts**:	Two movie theaters entered into an agreement to fix prices with each of eight film distributors. The contracts were individual to each distributor. There was no evidence of any agreement between distributors.

**Holding/Rationale:** There was a conspiracy among all eight movie distributors.

* Distributor is distributing movies to 8 movie theater chains ğŸ¡ª violation of the Sherman Act   
* Interstate sent out a letter to the 8 different theaters:   
  * Canâ€™t run double bills on movies  
  * Canâ€™t charge less than 25 cents   
* The court decides that it was enough that the distributors gave their adherence to the scheme and participated in it. Knowing that if the plan was carried out, it would result in a restraint of commerce, they all participated and renewed the arrangement and carried it into effect for the two successive years.

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Harcourt:** Spokes on a wheel. What is at stake here is to get distributors liable for the offense of other distributors (so making it a wheel).

## Mens Rea: Conspiracy  {#mens-rea:-conspiracy}

**Mens Rea**

* Knowledge \+ Intent/inferred intent   
* At common law, conspiracy is a specific intent crime.   
  * D must intend to agree with someone else.  
  * D must intend to commit the offense that is the object of the conspiracy. 

## People v. Lauria (CA., 1967\) â€“ when you can infer intent from knowledge (Mens Rea) {#people-v.-lauria-(ca.,-1967)-â€“-when-you-can-infer-intent-from-knowledge-(mens-rea)}

## â€œboth knowledge and intent are required for conspiracy mens rea. Intent can be inferred from knowledge of illegal activities.â€ {#â€œboth-knowledge-and-intent-are-required-for-conspiracy-mens-rea.-intent-can-be-inferred-from-knowledge-of-illegal-activities.â€}

**Rule**:	If a defendant did not take any direct action to further, encourage, or direct criminal activities, they cannot be charged with conspiracy.

**Facts**:	Lauria operated a telephone answering services for prostitutes to be connected with clients. Law enforcement was investigating the prostitutes and also went for the telephone service. Defendant knew some of his customers were prostitutes. Prosecutors wanted to prove knowledge of the prostitution.

**Holding/Rationale:** Lauria is not guilty of conspiracy because his knowledge/involvement didnâ€™t rise to the level of intent to participate in a criminal conspiracy.

* We do not believe an inference of intent drawn from knowledge of criminal use properly applies to the less serious crimes classified as misdemeanors.  
  * **Positive knowledge of the supplier that his products or services are being used for criminal purposes does not alone establish an intent of the supplier to participate in the misdemeanors.**

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* Can we infer intent from knowledge? In what circumstances can we infer intent from knowledge   
  * (1) purveyor of illegal goods has a stake in the venture  
  * (2) no legitimate use for the goods or services exist   
  * (3) volume of business with the buyer is grossly disproportionate to any legitimate demand or when sales for illegal use amount to a high proportion of the sellerâ€™s total business 

## Pinkerton Liability (liability for substantive offenses committed by co-conspirators) {#pinkerton-liability-(liability-for-substantive-offenses-committed-by-co-conspirators)}

Pinkerton liability allows an actor to be held liable for substantive crimes committed by his coconspirators in certain circumstances. A defendant can be held vicariously liable for a substantive offense committed by another member of a conspiracy

**Pros and Cons of Pinkerton**

* **For:** some make pragmatic arguments of the ability to impose costs on insulated â€œhigh-upâ€ individuals by exacting punishment against low-level conspirators. It might also serve as an incentive to provide information about high-level criminals.   
* **Against:** critics claim unfairness for penalizing one for acts they did not commit.    
  * The criticism of Pinkerton and Bridges applies in jurisdictions where accomplice liability requires culpability/intent to promote or facilities the specific offense the prosecution seeks to hold him accountable for.   
  * HOWEVER, many jurisdictions use an **objective foreseeability test** to determine the liability of an accomplice for originally unintended crimes committed by the principal in the course of the criminal endeavor, thereby producing results similar to Pinkerton (if not going further).  

**MPC rejects *Pinkerton* rule** 

* Under MPC, co-conspirator must satisfy the MPC elements for accessorial liability   
* Conspiracy by itself is not a basis for establishing complicity for all reasonably foreseeable substantive offenses committed in furtherance of the conspiracy 

## Pinkerton v. U.S. (CA., 1946\) â€“ liability for substantive offenses committed by a co-conspirator (**MIN RULE**) {#pinkerton-v.-u.s.-(ca.,-1946)-â€“-liability-for-substantive-offenses-committed-by-a-co-conspirator-(min-rule)}

## â€œliability if act in furtherance of conspiracy \+ within scope of the unlawful project \+ foreseeableâ€ {#â€œliability-if-act-in-furtherance-of-conspiracy-+-within-scope-of-the-unlawful-project-+-foreseeableâ€}

**Rule**:	You are liable for the substantive offense committed by a co-conspirator if:

* The act was in furtherance of the conspiracy;  
  * The act was within the scope of the unlawful project; AND  
  * The act was foreseeable as a necessary and natural consequence of the agreement


  **Facts**:	Pinkerton brothers live on a farm. They were indicted for tax-related violations. The indictment contains 10 substantive counts and 1 conspiracy count.


**Holding/Rationale:** There was enough evidence to submit to the jury the question of Danielâ€™s participation in the conspiracy 

* You donâ€™t need direct evidence of participation in the criminal conduct, as long as thereâ€™s a continuous conspiracy.   
* Acts of one conspirator may be attributed to the act of all.

We do not believe an inference of intent drawn from knowledge of criminal use properly applies to the less serious crimes classified as misdemeanors.

* **Positive knowledge of the supplier that his products or services are being used for criminal purposes does not alone establish an intent of the supplier to participate in the misdemeanors.**

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* How to deal with fact that we are holding someone liable for substantive counts when he doesnâ€™t even have knowledge of those acts   
  * Court rationalizes by imposing liability for foreseeable conduct. Def is being held liable for conspiracy, substantive counts in which he was aware, and those for which he was not aware so long as that was all foreseeable.   
* **Harcourt:** Notice proximity. Element of inchoateness in a conspiracy charge. Not all conspiracies need to come to fruition therefore you can be held liable for an agreement with an overt act but not actually with the result  
* **Stricter standard than MPC.** No mention on knowledge req for mens rea. Doesnâ€™t consider mens rea.  Hinges on foreseeability

## State v. bridges (NJ, 1993\) â€“ another application of pinkerton {#state-v.-bridges-(nj,-1993)-â€“-another-application-of-pinkerton}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-10}

**Rule**:	A co-conspirator is liable for the commission of substantive criminal acts that arenâ€™t within the scope of the conspiracy if they are reasonably foreseeable as the necessary or natural consequences of the conspiracy

**Facts**:	Defendant got into an argument with a party guest. He left to recruit two friends to help him fight the guy. They picked up guns on the way. At the party, Bridges got into a fight. A friend was struck in the tussle, and the friends began firing into the crowd, killing someone..

**Holding/Rationale:** A co-conspirator is liable for the commission of substantive criminal acts that arenâ€™t within the scope of the conspiracy if they are reasonably foreseeable as the necessary or natural consequences of the conspiracy.

* They brought loaded guns to a fight with several young, hostile partygoers from a beating of one of their friends.

## U.S. v. Alvarez (11th cir, 1985\) â€“ unprecedented application of pinkerton {#u.s.-v.-alvarez-(11th-cir,-1985)-â€“-unprecedented-application-of-pinkerton}

## â€œâ€ {#â€œâ€-11}

**Rule**:	Under the Pinkerton doctrine, a coconspirator can be liable for another coconspiratorâ€™s crime that was a reasonably foreseeable consequence of the conspiracy, even if the crime was outside the scope of the originally intended agreement.

**Facts**:	Undercover agents were in a motel room posing as buyers of a large quantity of cocaine. A shoot-out ensued, and one agent was killed. All the dealers were convicted of drug-related conspiracy. Three defendants were convicted of second-degree murder based on Pinkerton liability.

**Holding/Rationale:** Although the murder convictions of the three appellants may represent an unprecedented application of *Pinkerton*, such an application is not improper.

* There is plenty of evidence for a jury to conclude that the murder was a reasonably foreseeable consequence of the drug conspiracy.  
  * Though murder was not within originally intended scope of conspiracy (drug trafficking), it was reasonably foreseeable by Ds because deal involved large volume of drugs with high value.   
  * Ds must have been aware of the likelihood that   
    * 1\) at least some of their member would be carrying weapons, AND   
    * 2\) that deadly force would be used if necessary to protect the conspiratorsâ€™ interests.   
  * Ds played a significant part in the transaction, such as acting as a lookout, introducing the principles and being present during some of the negotiations   
    * **Rule is not retroactive**; a person who joins a conspiracy is not responsible for crimes already committed by co-conspirators 

**Important Info/ Class Notes**

* **Takeaway:** *Pinkerton* can lead to a lot of liability.
